==PAGE:6==
==PAGE:7==
Abstract
The aim of this thesis is to characterise fatigue damage observed in rolling
element bearings under conditions of high load and slippage. A test rig con-
structed for rolling contact fatigue tests of rings is described, and results are
presented for inner rings collected from spherical roller bearings. The ana-
lytical considerations of Hertzian contact and Hamrock simpliﬁed solutions
needed to perform the tests are elaborated. The preparation of the test rings
and the loading situation are explained. Test conditions are chosen with the
aim of achieving pitting formation at the contacting surfaces. During testing
the contact pressure, torque and the rotational speed are monitored and re-
corded. To compare time to failure of the tests a Weibull probability plot is
utilised. After testing, the inner rings have been characterised using X-ray
tomography and scanning electron microscopy. An introduction to contact
modelling in COMSOL Multiphysics® as well as a simpliﬁed 2D model si-
mulating a test is presented. The observations conﬁrm that rolling contact
fatigue testing at high loads leads to pitting failure at the contacting surfaces
however further tests are needed to describe the eﬀect of slippage on rolling
contact fatigue.
Keywords
100Cr6 steel · Bearing failure · Numerical calculations · Rolling
contact fatigue · Test rig · Weibull probability plot

Resumé (Danish)
Formålet med denne kandidatafhandling er at karakterisere udmattelsesska-
der i lejer under høj belastning og glidning. En test rig konstrueret til rul-
lekontaktudmattelse af lejeringe er beskrevet, og resultaterne præsenteres for
indvendige lejeringe fra sfæriske rullelejer. De analytiske overvejelser af Hertz
kontaktteori og Hamrocks forenklede løsninger, der er nødvendige for at udføre
testene, er præsenteret. Forberedelsen af testringene og belastningssituatio-
nen forklares. Testbetingelser vælges med det formål at opnå pittingdannelse
ved kontaktﬂaderne. Under testene overvåges og registreres kontakttrykket,
drejningsmomentet og rotationshastigheden. For at sammenligne levetiden af
testene anvendes et Weibull sandsynlighedsplot. Efter testene er de indvendige
ringe blevet karakteriseret ved hjælp af røntgentomograﬁog scanningelektron-
mikroskopi. En introduktion til kontaktmodellering i COMSOL Multiphysics®
samt en forenklet 2D-model, der simulerer en test, præsenteres. Observati-
onerne bekræfter, at rullekontaktudmattelse ved høje belastninger fører til
pittingdannelse ved kontaktﬂaderne, men yderligere test er nødvendige for at
beskrive eﬀekten af glidning på rullekontaktudmattelse.
Nøgleord
100Cr6 stål · Lejersvigt · Numeriske beregninger · Rullekontak-
tudmattelse · Test rig · Weibull sandsynlighedsplot

Preface
The thesis is drafted during the ﬁrst seven months of 2017 as the ﬁnal pro-
ject to achieve the degree of Master of Sciences in Mechanical Engineering
(M.Sc.Eng.)
at the Technical University of Denmark (DTU). The thesis
work represents 35 ECTS points of the 120 ECTS points required to achieve
M.Sc.Eng.
The project was carried out at DTU in Kgs. Lyngby and was supervised by
professor Peder Klit from DTU Mechanical Engineering the Department of
Mechanical Engineering, Section of Solid Mechanics.
I would like to express special thanks to my supervisor Peder Klit who always
provided help and advice whenever I needed it. I would also like to thank the
project partners from DTU Wind Energy the Department of Wind Energy
at DTU Risø; Dorte Juul Jensen, Søren Fæster, Anand Natarajan and Oleg
Mishin, for co-authoring the symposium paper, providing insight and suggesti-
ons during our meetings and for providing the funds for modifying the test rig.
In addition, I would like to thank SKF Denmark for sponsoring 40 bearings
to be used as test pieces for the thesis work.
Further thanks to Joseﬁne, Alex, Tonny and Simon, who kindly read through
an initial version of the thesis and provided comments and suggestions for
improvements of the text.
Kgs. Lyngby, 4th of August 2017
Kristian Tolmose Hansen

1 Introduction
1.1
Background
Bearings in wind turbines fail prematurely. For instance, the main bearing
in wind turbines will frequently fail within 10 −15 % of the intended turbine
lifetime meaning that the main bearing should be changed every second to
third year [1]. Premature bearing failure reduces the sustainability of wind
turbines in general. The failure creates unwanted service costs for the compa-
nies producing or owning the wind turbines. The expenses are especially high
for oﬀshore wind turbines as specialised crane vessels are needed to remove
the turbine blades to service the main bearing or to change the gearbox.
Figure 1.1: (a) Pitting formation from surface defect (b) spalling initiation from
subsurface defect. Figure 1.2 from Cerullo [2]
A wear mechanism often observed in a premature failed main bearing in wind
turbines is so-called pitting. This wear mechanism is not driven by a general
overloading of the bearing, but rather a combination of several factors. First
of all the rollers and raceway contact each other at asperities on both surfaces
creating high stresses in the surfaces. This contact is established as a result
of the lubrication ﬁlm not being thick enough to separate the two surfaces
suﬃciently. Secondly, shear stresses are introduced at the raceway due to slip
between the surfaces. The slip brings the maximum stress closer to the surfa-
ces, thus increasing the speed at which material is worn by the surfaces [3]. In
1
==PAGE:22==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
Figure 1.1 a schematic representation of the pitting mechanism is compared
to the spalling mechanism which occurs in well-lubricated bearings without
surface defects. The mechanism causing the above-described failures is called
rolling Contact Fatigue (RCF).
The aim of this thesis is to support the REWIND project by investigating
RCF in rolling element bearings. The particular area of interest is how slip
between rolling elements and bearing rings aﬀects RCF. This is done via RCF
tests performed on bearing steel. The REWIND project is a research centre
established in January 2011. The research centre consists of 12 partners and
employs 11 PhDs and 5 post docs. The aim of the research centre is to "perform
strategic research at the highest level in the ﬁeld of material-manufacturing-
properties-performance of metallic components in the rotor and drive train in
large wind turbines, with the ultimate aim of enhancing the reliability and
arriving at an improved life expectancy prediction of such components." [1].
1.2
Structure of the Thesis
The project hypothesis is that slip introduced between two rolling elements
will cause the critical shear stresses in the material to move closer to the con-
tact surface. The critical shear stress will thus cause pitting damage to occur
faster as the cracks will need to propagate a shorter distance.
This thesis has been drafted to investigate the project hypothesis. In this
section, the structure of the thesis will brieﬂy be elaborated:
• Chapter 2: Basic concepts of physical mechanisms which are fundamen-
tal to the project is introduced.
• Chapter 3: The design of the test rig is elaborated in detail.
• Chapter 4: The analytical framework needed to perform and evaluate
the tests are elaborated.
• Chapter 5: A presentation of a test run and the performed tests are
described.
• Chapter 6: Numerical modelling in COMSOL Multiphysics® is introdu-
ced to facilitate the creation of models describing the load situation.
• Chapter 7: Results gathered through the project are presented and dis-
cussed.
• Chapter 8: The thesis is concluded, and ideas for future work are pre-
sented.
==PAGE:23==
2 Basic Theory
In this section, the important basic theory and concepts applied during the
thesis will be introduced. The most common known concepts will be brieﬂy
introduced, while the less known concepts will be described in greater detail.
2.1
Strength of Materials
The strength theory is the basis for performing calculations on the behaviour
of solid materials in diﬀerent mechanical loading conditions. It provides basic
measures, relating the external load of a mechanical structure to the internal
load in the material as well as describing the eﬀect of the load on the structure.
When an external force loads a mechanical structure, the material has to
match the force to carry the load, thus creating internal forces in the structure
[4]. If the material is homogeneous and the point being investigated is far from
the loading points, the internal forces can be assumed to cover the entire cross
sectional area of the structure. As examined in Figure 2.1 a homogeneous bar
split into two equal sized pieces with equal but opposite orientated external
forces, P, must have opposing internal forces, N, of the same magnitude as P
to stay in equilibrium.
N −P = 0

The cut plane B −C is located far from the external load P; it can thus be
assumed that the internal forces are equally distributed over the entire cross
section. This assumption makes it possible to describe the internal forces as
the normal stress, σ, times the cross sectional area, A.
N = σA
The unit of σ is force divided by area, and σ follows the normal of the cut
plane. To describe stress which is not normal to the cut plane another stress
term is needed: the shear stress, τ, which also has the unit, force divided
by area. The shear stress runs parallel to the cut plane. A new cut plane
D −E is introduced. As shown in Figure 2.2 the cut plane is angled ϕ with
respect to the load direction of P. To describe the new case, a stress vector
s is introduced which consists of two components σϕ normal to the cut plane
and τϕ parallel to the cut plane.

The stress components show that stresses must be described in a point with

When a material is stressed enough, it will deform. The ﬁrst deformation
stage is elastic deformation where the structure will return to its original shape
when unloaded. The second stage of deformation is plastic deformation. At
this stage, the shape of the structure is altered permanently when the load is
removed. The elastic/plastic deformation regions are illustrated in Figure 2.3
using a tensile test specimen as an example.

Figure 2.3: Illustrating the diﬀerent stages of a stressed specimen as it is elongated.
Inspired by Figure 2.1b in Kalpakjian and Schmid [6].
Figure 2.3 illustrates a specimen with an original length, lo, being elastically
deformed to the length le. The main part of the deformation is linear elas-
tic which means that the stress and strain are proportional to each other as
shown in the linear part of the curve. As the material is stretched close to the
yield stress, YS, the material is no longer linear elastic, but will still return
to the original shape when unloaded. When loading past the YS limit, plas-
tic deformation occurs. This is shown as the cross sectional area is reduced
uniformly along the length of the rod lu. If the load is removed past the YS
point, the curve will follow a straight line parallel to the original linear elastic
curve. The YS point has thus moved further up the curve, and the material
has become harder. When the specimen is loaded past the ultimate tensile
strength, UTS, a so-called neck will start to form, as illustrated in Figure 2.3.
The cross sectional area will be reduced quickly in the necking area hence
reducing the stress. Ultimately the thinnest point of the neck will fracture at
length lF [6].
The main focus of this thesis will be the linear elastic deformation as most
mechanical elements are loaded in the elastic zone during their lifetime. When
deformations can be considered small, and the material is in the linear elastic
region the relation between stress and strain can be described by Hook’s law:
σ = Eε

2.1.1
Hoop Stresses
Hoop stresses are normal stresses in the circumferential direction in a cylin-
drical coordinate system. Hoop stress is denoted σθθ and shown in Figure 2.5
[5].
Figure 2.5: Section of a rotationally symmetric object in a cylindrical coordinate
system. Figure 10.1 from Tvergaard et al. [5]
The hoop stress is deﬁned as the force working on the sectioned wall area and
will grow when moving closer to the inside diameter of the ring because there
is less material.
2.2
Hardness
Hardness is a property which is used to determine a material’s resistance to
permanent indentation. Hardness thus describes how well material is at resis-
ting scratches in the surface and wear. Steel is harder than aluminium, while
steel is softer than diamond and within diﬀerent steel grades, the hardness dif-
fers as well. However the hardness of a material is not a fundamental property
- it depends on the shape of the indenter and how the load is applied. The-
refore diﬀerent hardness scales are used to further specify the exact hardness
properties of a material [6].
2.2.1
Hardness Scale Types
The most common used hardness scales are brieﬂy introduced in this section.
The diﬀerent indenter types, as well as the diﬀerent hardnesses calculations,
are shown in Table 2.1.

Brinell
When performing the Brinell hardness test a 10 mm steel ball is
pressed into a surface. For materials harder than 500 Brinell Hardness (HB)
a tungsten-carbide ball is used instead of steel ball because it has a higher
modulus of elasticity. HB is deﬁned as a ratio between the load applied, the
diameter of the ball used and the diameter of the indentation. The ratio is
shown in Table 2.1 along with the loads and the shape of the indenter [6].
==PAGE:30==
10
CHAPTER 2. BASIC THEORY
Vickers
A Vickers hardness test is performed by creating an indentation
with a pyramid shaped diamond indenter as shown in Table 2.1. The measu-
rement shown in the table is performed, and the hardness is calculated. The
test usually creates small indentations of 0.5 mm diagonally. This hardness
test is suitable for materials with a broad range of hardnesses, including heat
treated steels, as the test yields roughly the same hardness for varying loads
[6].
Rockwell
S.P. Rockwell developed a hardness test which measures the depth
of indentation rather than the diameter as seen in other tests. The Rockwell
hardness (HR(A, B, C, D, E, F or G)) is obtained by ﬁrst creating an indenta-
tion with a light load and then another using a primary load. The diﬀerence
in depth is then used as the measure of hardness. The most common Rockwell
indenters, as well as loads used, can be found in Table 2.1 [6].
2.2.2
Hardenability
Hardenability is the term used to describe the ability of a material to be harde-
ned. It is the measure of the hardening depth achieved by heat treatment, and
should not be confused with the magnitude of the actual hardness achieved.
The hardenability of a ferrous material is dependent on the carbon content,
the grain size of austenite, the presence of alloy elements and the cooling rate.
A test used to measure the hardenability of material is the Jominy test. To
perform the test a 100 mm long rod made from the material to be investiga-
ted, is heated to form 100 % austenite and then quenched by subjecting one
end to a water jet of 24°C. The cooling rate will thus vary along the length
of the rod, resulting in decreasing hardness further from the water jet. When
the rod is cooled the hardness is tested along the entire length of the rod in
an interval of 1.5 mm between each measurement for alloyed steels or 0.75
mm for carbon steels. The typical hardness scales used for the Jominy test is
either Rockwell or Vickers [6].
2.2.3
Hardening Processes
Through-hardening processes harden a component uniformly throughout the
thickness. These processes are often used for small, and mass produced parts,
as it is hard solely to heat the surface of a small component to achieve dif-
ferent hardnesses during quenching. The disadvantage of through-hardening
is that the material hardens throughout the whole thickness, which makes
it more brittle. A crack in the surface can thus propagate quickly through
the part. Hardening processes which only aﬀect the surface are thus desired.
Processes which only aﬀect the surface can be characterised as case-hardening
because only the "case" is hardened.
The underlying material will still be
soft and ductile after quenching.
An example of a case-hardening process
==PAGE:31==
2.3. FATIGUE
11
is induction-hardening. Induction heating is a process that utilises electro-
magnetic induction to generate heat in the workpiece. The workpiece must
be made from a conductive material for heating to take place.
Induction-
hardening only hardens the case of a part. The process transforms some of
the heated material to martensite, increasing the hardness and brittleness of
the component. Induction can be used to heat a speciﬁc part of the compo-
nent, the bearing raceway, for instance, to produce a precise hardening that
does not aﬀect the underlying material [6].
2.3
Fatigue
2.3.1
Classical Fatigue
Mechanical components eventually fail due to a variety of reasons. Overloa-
ding or corrosion is often seen in failed or failing components. In these cases,
the cause of failure is easily determined. However, some components fail at
signiﬁcantly lower loads than expected. These components have been sub-
jected to cyclic stresses which lead to fatigue of the material. In some of these
cases, it is not easy to specify the cause of fatigue.
Fatigue failure begins as an insigniﬁcant defect in the material. As time passes
the defect grows due to continuously loading and unloading until a fracture
occurs [6].
Fatigue cracks are often characterised by being transgranular,
straight, unramiﬁed and wide. Only a few of these cracks are seen in a pure
fatigue failure.
A fatigue fracture is typically brittle.
Consequently, it is
sometimes possible to ﬁnd the spot at which the crack started and thereby
determine how the crack progressed [7].
For some materials such as steel, fatigue fracture will not occur when the load
is kept under the fatigue load limit. This limit is seen in a Wöler (S/N) curve
when the curve ﬂattens. Not all materials have this limit and will continue
downwards until a fracture occurs. One of these materials is aluminium.
2.3.2
Rolling Contact Fatigue
In a rolling element bearing the classical way of approaching the fatigue life
has been to compare the load with the operation time. However, this is not
adequate for modern bearings as they, given the right conditions, can operate
for far longer and their failure does not only originate from the load. When
running a bearing free from contamination with contact pressures governed by
Hertzian contact theory, the accepted point of fatigue initiation is at the point
of maximum shear stress below the surface. The maximum orthogonal shear
stress is for instance located 0.5b below the surface for a line contact (Figure
4.3), where b is the contact semi-width. At this point, microcracks begin to
==PAGE:32==
12
CHAPTER 2. BASIC THEORY
form, and with continued operation, they grow until a piece of material breaks
oﬀ, forming a pit on the surface as shown in Figure 2.8. Continuing to operate
a bearing with pits will eventually lead to ﬂaking. Flaking is a phenomenon
where small pieces of the surface peel oﬀ, leaving a rough surface. As ﬂaking
spreads across the surface, the operating noise will increase.
Figure 2.8: Pits seen on the contact surface of test ring 8-1.
The reason why cracks traditionally start at the point of maximum shear
stress is inhomogeneities in the material, such as small inclusions or unevenly
distributed alloy elements. However, in modern bearing steel, the material
cleanliness is much improved. Crack initiation can still occur at inhomoge-
neities in the material but has to some extent moved to indentations in the
surface. When a foreign particle in the lubrication is compressed between the
bearing components, a small indentation is created. The indentation will to
some extent look like the indentation made in a work-hardened metal shown
in Figure 2.6. The raised edges of the indention will cause local peak stresses
to occur every time a rolling element passes, which leads to the formation of
a crack that will grow to the point of maximum shear stress. From here the
crack will spread like classical fatigue damage (Figure 1.1). The cleanliness of
the lubricant is thus as important as ever. In Figure 2.9 it is illustrated that
harder contamination particles in the lubrication will have a greater impact
than softer particles. This is due to harder materials being more likely to
produce indentations in the surface.
==PAGE:33==
2.3. FATIGUE
13
Figure 2.9: Reduction in lifetime due to contamination of the lubricant. Figure 7.5
from Brändlein et al. [8]
.
If the oil ﬁlm is not suﬃcient to separate the rolling surfaces, sliding can occur
between them. The sliding will result in crack formation moving closer to the
surface, thus creating pitting faster. As material breaks oﬀfrom the surface
and mixes with the lubrication, indentations will form on the surface, resulting
in damage as seen with external contamination. If the material breaking oﬀ
is suﬃciently large critical overloads can occur, leading to ring fracture.
A type of wear often seen in bearings with line contacts is distinct lines run-
ning parallel to the rolling direction or a distinct change in smoothness in the
rolling contact. The cause of this is uneven wear on the rollers causing the
load to be shifted onto a narrow part of the roller. The decrease in contact
surface increases the contact pressure signiﬁcantly, creating peak stresses in
the material.
When fatigue damage is progressing in a bearing, its running behaviour will
deviate from normal - the vibration level and operating noise will be signiﬁ-
cantly higher. These conditions originate from rolling elements not running on
a smooth surface. Common causes for premature fatigue in a rolling element
bearing are listed below.
• Larger than anticipated loading of the bearing.
• Poor lubrication.
– Insuﬃcient lubrication.
– Contamination in the lubrication.
– Water in the lubrication.
• Incorrect installation of bearing leading to excessive preloading [8].

3 Experimental Setup
The purpose of the experiments is to investigate the eﬀect of slip in RCF.
The principle of the tests is to force two rotating rings together until failure.
The test rig which is shown in Figure 3.1 was created to achieve this. To
produce the slip Drive 1 is set to rotate faster than Drive 2 thus transferring
a torque through the contact and creating shear forces within the material
in and around the contact zone. As pitting occurs, the mechanical test is
stopped, and the test rings are then characterised using 3D X-ray tomography
microscope and scanning electron microscopy (SEM). This characterization is
later used to compare the RCF tests to the numerical calculations and the
analytical analysis.
Figure 3.1: Test rig as seen from the end at which the hydraulic loading arrangement
is mounted.
15
==PAGE:36==
16
CHAPTER 3. EXPERIMENTAL SETUP
3.1
Test Rig Design
In this section, the individual components of the test rig shown in Figure 3.1
are outlined. The lower part of the test rig is an old lathe which provides a
solid base for clamping down other parts of the setup. The lathe is equipped
with linear rails which are used both to clamp down the diﬀerent parts of the
assembly, as well as provide a way to move the shafts away from each other
for easy removal. A drawing of the lathe is shown in Figure 3.2.
Figure 3.2: Drawing is showing the base of a lathe used for the test rig. Figure 2.4
From Janakiraman [1].
Two heavy steel sections were made to carry the shafts holding the test pieces
and ﬁx them to the lathe. One section is created to quickly move back and
forth on the rails when the shafts are to be removed or put back into the test
rig (Figure 3.3a). The other steel section (Figure 3.3b) is also mounted on
rails, but this section is intended to be ﬁxed at all times. This ﬁxture can pivot
around a point close to the rails. A hydraulic loading arrangement mounted
diagonally opposite the pivot point controls the pivoted position of the ﬁxture
as shown in Figure 3.5.
==PAGE:37==
3.1. TEST RIG DESIGN

Connecting the ﬁxtures with the shafts are four spherical roller bearings of the
type SKF Explorer 22207 E. These bearings are pressed onto the shafts and
held in place by lock nuts. The assembled shafts are then clamped onto the
ﬁxtures by large steel clamps. The axial position of the shafts are controlled by
shim washers of varying thickness, a wave spring per shaft, and end covers to
hold the assembly together. A schematic representation of the parts mentioned
above assembled and pushed into contact is shown in Figure 3.4. A section
view of the assembled components is shown in Figure 3.5.

on Drive 2 into contact with Drive 1 as the ﬁxture is pivoted directly below

a hand operated pump, an accumulator, an analogue pressure gauge and a

(a) Closeup of the hand pump

==PAGE:39==
3.1. TEST RIG DESIGN
19
The external force applied to the contact must be calculated to achieve the
desired contact pressure.
The calculations to determine the external force
will be introduced in Section 4.2. The exact calculations will be elaborated
in Section 4.4.2. With the desired external force calculated it is possible to
calculate the corresponding pressure in the hydraulic loading arrangement.
These calculations will be elaborated in Sections 4.4.3.
Powering Drive 1 and 2 are two diﬀerent electric motors. The slave motor
on Drive 1 is a 5.5 kW Klee Drive IE2 three phase induction motor which is
controlled by a Danfoss Drive VLT 2800 three phase frequency converter. To
absorb input power via the shaft the slave motor is connected to a radiator
which converts the excess electricity to heat. The master motor on Drive 2 is a
5.5 kW Danfoss Drive VLT FCM 300 IE2 three phase induction motor likewise
controlled by a Danfoss Drive VLT 2800 frequency converter and is monitored
by an LCP remote connected to the frequency converter. The master motor
is not able to absorb power via the shaft; thus it must always have equal or
larger rotational speed than the slave motor to avoid damaging the motor.
The frequency of each drive can be varied from 0 Hz to 50 Hz to enable both
rolling and sliding conditions.
To monitor the operation of the test rig and ensure that each drive is perfor-
ming as expected, a monitoring program created for the test rig in LabVIEW
is used. The program records and displays the following values: time, pressure
in the hydraulic loading arrangement, torque on Drive 2 as well as its average
value, rotational speed of Drive 1 and 2 as well as their average values and
the total number of revolutions of each drive. The recorded values are stored
in a data ﬁle for later data treatment. Pressure in the hydraulic loading ar-
rangement is measured between the accumulator and the hand pump with a
P8AP absolute pressure transducer made by HBM shown in the top of Figure
3.6a. Torque is measured with a DR-2112 torque sensor on Drive 2, and the
rotational speed of each drive is measured using micro photo sensors of the
type EE-SX461-P11.
To lubricate and cool the contacting rings two small pipes are positioned
roughly 2 cm above the contact. One pipe directs oil into the contact, while
the other is placed over the ring on Drive 1 letting the oil run along the ring
into the contact. The oil is not pressurised and runs freely into the contact
after which it is sprayed around providing lubrication for the bearings carrying
the shafts. A drip tray is placed below the lathe to collect the oil, and via a
pipe connected to the oil reservoir. A 70-micron ﬁlter and an LOC2 air oil
cooler are likewise connected oﬄine to the oil reservoir. The ﬁltration system,
as well as the cooling system, are switched on during operation to ensure the
best oil quality. The lubrication system is shown in Figure 3.7.
==PAGE:40==

3.2
Test Rings
The test rig was initially employed by Shravan Janakiraman during his PhD
work [1]. It was designed to perform lubricated RFC testing on ring shaped
test pieces under varying conditions. Janakiraman used the test rig to inves-
tigate the lubricating eﬀect of grooves in the rolling surface of the test piece
as well as studying hydrogen embrittlement in the test piece by running them
until ring fracture. The test pieces used by Janakiraman was ring shaped and
made speciﬁcally for the tests. One of these test rings is shown in Figure 3.8a.
While performing his tests, the test pieces were plastically deformed by rolling
between the shafts thus changing the shape of the rings, which aﬀected the
ﬁt as well as the contact pressure. This plastic deformation is to be avoided
in this present work, as the ﬁt between the shaft and the ring must be con-
stant and the contact pressure must be as constant as possible. Thus, new
shafts have been made to accommodate the use of inner rings from spherical
roller bearings as test pieces. The bearings used are Explorer 21311 EK + H
311 spherical roller bearings supplied by SKF. These bearings are comparable
with current wind turbine bearings. One of these new test pieces is shown in
Figure 3.8b.
==PAGE:41==
3.2. TEST RINGS
21

(b) The type of test ring used in

Figure 3.8: Original and new test pieces for the test rig.
The material composition and hardness of the inner rings of the bearings tested
are identical to those found in actual wind turbines. A schematic cross section
of the bearing is shown in Figure 3.9a. In Figure 3.9b the intended contact
established between the two rings when mounted in the test rig is shown. The
contact surface was ground, to make one of the rings wider. When comparing
old and new test pieces seen in Figure 3.8, the contact surface on the new
test piece is more narrow in the axial direction, thus enabling a larger contact
pressure with a reduced external force compared to earlier. The reduced force
along with the diﬀerent shape of the outer surface of the rings is assumed to
prevent plastic deformation of the ring. The new test pieces are mounted on
the shafts using cone interference ﬁts. A frustum with the half cone angle
2.386° ± 0.02° is made on the new shafts to match the frustum in the bearing
rings. Using cone interference ﬁts causes hoop stresses to form in the test
pieces which also occurs in bearings operating in a wind turbine. The hoop
stresses can be varied depending on the distance the ring is pressed onto the
frustum.

3.3
Materials
In this section, the materials directly aﬀected during the tests are outlined.
3.3.1
Bearing Steel
A bearing is aﬀected by many diﬀerent factors during its lifetime to achieve
a product that can fulﬁl its purpose satisfactorily and ensure a long lifetime.
These factors must be taken into account when choosing materials for a be-
aring. The diﬀerent parts of a bearing have various purposes and thus have
diﬀerent material requirements. Bearing rings and rolling elements have simi-
lar requirements and are thus made from the same material. The cage and
caps are made from other materials as these parts experience diﬀerent loads
during their lifetime. The area of interest for this thesis is the material and
hardening methods applied to the bearing rings and rolling elements. The
primary purpose of these parts is to carry the load applied to the bearing.
However, resistance against rolling contact fatigue and dimensional stability
of the bearing as a whole are desired. To achieve the load carrying capacity
required of the bearing the bearing rings and rolling elements are hardened.
Depending on the intended application of the bearing, diﬀerent hardening
processes are applied. Through-hardening is used for small bearings, and in-
duction hardening is used for larger bearings. To some extent, the hardening
process that has been chosen dictates which material is suited for the bearing.
Materials for bearings are produced and hardened following ISO 683-17 which
is the standard for bearing steels [9].
The ISO 683-17 standard speciﬁes what level of quality the material must
meet to be satisfactory for the customer. These speciﬁcations include heat-
treatment, surface properties and ﬁnish, traceability to the particular casting,
==PAGE:43==
3.3. MATERIALS
23
chemical composition, hardenability, hardness, the microstructural grain size,
non-metallic inclusions and the general shape and dimensions of the work
piece. Included are methods for inspection and tests as well as tables that
specify the criteria mentioned above [10].
SKF has stated that the Explorer 21311 EK + H 311 spherical roller bearings
used in the experiments have the following material properties:
• Compressive stresses in the surface zone: 50 to 125 MPa
• Surface hardness: 61 to 62 HRC
• Core hardness: 61 HRC
• 7 to 12 % tougher than standard Bainite steels
According to SKF [9, p. 275] a bearing with a shaft diameter d ≤80 mm
can be considered small. The Explorer 21311 has a shaft diameter of d = 55
mm and is thus small. Through-hardening is a bainitic heat treatment which
according to SKF [9, p.
151] gives a hardness between 58 and 65 HRC.
Considering the facts mentioned above the bearing rings and rollers in the
Explorer 21311 have been through-hardened. A commonly used bearing steel
for through-hardening is 100Cr6. It has the chemical composition shown in
Table 3.1 and the mechanical properties shown in Table 3.2.
Table 3.1: Chemical composition of 100Cr6 bearing steel [10, Table 3].

3.3.3
Oil
The primary purpose of the oil is to cool the test rings during operation. Anot-
her but important task is to lubricate the spherical roller bearings supporting
the shafts in the test rig. The oil used during the testing was a full synthetic
heavy duty gear oil: Mobil Delvac Synthetic Gear oil 75W-140, data sheet
included in Appendix 8.1. The oil has a viscosity of 182 cSt at 40°C and 25
cSt at 100°C. The oil was not changed before testing, as the quality of the oil
seemed to be in good condition and the lubricating ability of the oil was not
strictly essential for the test itself. As such the lubrication is not included in
any calculations.
==PAGE:45==
4 Analytical Calculations
In this chapter, the basic theory needed to perform analysis of bearing steel
and the analytical calculations to determine the operating parameters is des-
cribed. The theory is mainly based on Hamrock’s approach described in his
book "Fundamentals of Fluid Film Lubrication, second edition" [12]. The book
"Contact mechanics" by Johnson [13] has been used as well for calculating the
initial conditions for the tests in which two rotating rings are pressed together,
to create a line contact. Calculations to determine how a bearing would be
aﬀected by the load applied in the tests are also presented in this chapter.
4.1
Hertzian Contact
When an initial contact is formed between two non-conforming surfaces, they
only touch at one single point or a line. Immediately when an external load is
applied both surfaces will begin to deform, thus creating a contact area. The
shape and size of a contact area depend on the initial contact, the curvature
of the surfaces, the elastic properties of the material as well as the load di-
rection. The shape will, however, be either elliptical or rectangular, creating
two boundary cases. When the principal radii of curvature shown in Figure
4.1 are rax = ray and rbx = rby the contact area becomes circular, and when
ray = rby = ∞the contact area becomes rectangular. The theory of contact
mechanics is created to describe how contact area changes with the magnitude
and direction of the load as well as the corresponding directions of stresses
and strains in the vicinity of the contact [13].
4.1.1
Geometry Description
To formulate the elastic problem a description of the geometry is needed. Both
contacting non-conforming surfaces are considered perfectly smooth to create
this description. Both surfaces are ellipsoids with diﬀerent radii in the x- and
y-directions. A x-y plane is deﬁned as the common tangent plane through the
contact point, with the positive z-axis going into the lower surface. The origin
of this x-y plane is located in the original contact point.
25
==PAGE:46==
26
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTICAL CALCULATIONS
Figure 4.1: Two solid ellipsoids contacting each other at one point. Figure 17.1
from Hamrock [12].
In Figure 4.1 an external normal load wz is forcing the two solids together.
The diﬀerent principal radii of curvature rax, ray, rbx and rby are shown. Note
that each principal radius has diﬀerent centres, indicating that the solids are
ellipsoids. The x- and y-coordinates are chosen to satisfy Equation 4.1. When
the contact is convex (Figure 4.1) the contact is positive whereas a concave
contact is negative. This means that if the centre of curvature for a principal
radius is within the solid, that principal radius is positive, and if the centre is
outside the solid, the principal radius is negative.

4.1.2
Contact Formulation
While using two glass lenses to study Newton’s optical interference fringes,
Hertz became interested in the inﬂuence of elastic deformation of the surfaces
due to the contact pressure. Because of this, he formulated a theory of elastic
contact from static loading which has been the basis for much work within
contact mechanics until this day.
From investigating two non-conforming glass lenses, Hertz hypothesised that
in general, contact area is elliptical. He simpliﬁed that both solids can be
assumed as elastic half spaces which are only loaded in a small plane elliptical
region. By making this simpliﬁcation, it is possible to calculate the highly con-
centrated contact stresses separately from general stresses in the two solids.
Hertz’s simpliﬁcation makes it possible to use the developed methods for sol-
ving boundary value problems in half spaces. Some conditions are needed to
use the simpliﬁcation. The contact semi-width b must be narrow compared to
the overall dimensions of the solids in contact and b must be narrow compared
to the eﬀective radii of curvature. These conditions ensure that calculations
are not signiﬁcantly inﬂuenced by the proximity to any boundaries and that
the contact ellipse is roughly planar as assumed. Furthermore, the conditions
ensure that strains in the contact are within the boundaries of linear elasticity.
A ﬁnal assumption is that no friction occurs in the contact thus only normal
forces are transmitted through the contact [13].
Hamrock has used the theory created by Hertz to describe the contact between
the two solids shown in Figure 4.1. Hamrock summarises the assumptions as
follows:
• The materials are homogeneous, and the yield stress is not exceeded.
==PAGE:48==
28
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTICAL CALCULATIONS
• No tangential forces are induced between the solids.
• Contact is limited to a small portion of the surface such that the dimen-
sions of the contact region are small in comparison with the radii of the
ellipsoids.
• The solids are at rest and in equilibrium.
Hertz considered the initial contact between two elliptical solids as a point
contact. This point contact will always be a small contact area due to defor-
mation of the surface. Equation 4.7 is created from the above assumptions
and describes the contact pressure within the ellipsoidal contact.
p = pmax

4.1.3
Subsurface Stress
Fatigue cracks are often seen nucleating below the surface as maximum shear
stress, τ0, is reached below the surface. This crack nucleation is a commonly
known phenomenon and has been investigated by Johnson [13] who approx-
imated that the maximum principal shear stress is 0.31pmax at a depth of
0.48b for a circular contact area. The expressions shown in this section are
from Hamrock et al. [12] and are based on the analysis by Lundberg and
Palmgren.
Lundberg and Palmgren described the position and magnitude of the maxi-
mum shear stress in the x-y-z-coordinate system established in Section 4.1.1.
They assumed y = 0 considering the problem in the z-x-plane; the x-direction
being the rolling direction and the positive z-axis going into the surface. The
Equations 4.17 to 4.19 are from their analysis.

4.2
Hamrock Solution
Hamrock et al. [12] has made simpliﬁed versions of the classical Hertzian
solution to the elastic deformation of contacting solids. The Hertzian solution
requires the calculation of the ellipticity parameter k and complete elliptic
integrals of the ﬁrst and second kinds F and E, whereas Brewe and Hamrock
in 1977 devised a solution which is a function of the radius ratio αr. This
solution was however only fairly accurate in the range 1.0 ≤αr ≤100. In
the 1983-solution Brewe and Hamrock reﬁned their work to get an eﬀective
range of 0.03 ≤αr ≤100, thus being able to describe a disk rolling on a plane
(αr = 0.03) as well as a line contact (αr →100). The literature used for this
section is Hamrock et al. [12, Ch. 17].
4.2.1
Simpliﬁed Solution
While creating the simpliﬁed solution Brewe and Hamrock compared their
work to an iterative numerical solution created by Hamrock and Anderson
in 1973, which is considered exact. Instead of using the iterative numerical
approach Brewe and Hamrock used the method of least squares. This method
is a linear regression to powerﬁt a set of pairs of data; k, αr.
The linear
regression resulted in the following equation:

4.3
Rolling Element Bearing Calculations
To compare the lifetime of the test rings to an actual bearing lifetime, cal-
culations to determine the load on the heaviest loaded rolling element in a
bearing is performed. The basis for these calculations is Hamrock et al. [12,
Ch. 17 and 21]. In Chapter 21 Hamrock brieﬂy describes the history of the
rolling element bearing, the diﬀerent types of bearings, their geometry as well
as how they are loaded and eventually fail.
==PAGE:54==
34
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTICAL CALCULATIONS
4.3.1
Heaviest Loaded Roller
The basic form of the equation used to calculate the load on the heaviest
loaded rolling element is given in Equation 4.41.
The denominator in the
equation was initially calculated by Stribeck in 1901 for ball bearings with
zero diametral clearance cd. Equation 4.41 can, however, provide an initial
estimate of the load before performing further calculations.
wz = nR (wz)max
4.37
(4.41)
In this equation nR is the number of rolling elements in the bearing, (wz)max
is the highest load on a single rolling element and 4.37 is a constant originally
denoted Zw for ball bearings. Zw relates diametral clearance in the unloaded
bearing to the local elastic compression, δmax, of the rolling element in the
loaded bearing. Equation 4.41 is rewritten to the form in Equation 4.42 giving
the desired result for further calculations.
(wz)max = 4.37wz
nR
(4.42)
For the bearing used in this thesis, the total number of rollers are 38. However
as it is desired just to investigate the contact pressure between one roller and
the bearing rings, only one row of rollers are considered thus nR = 19. The
radial load wz applied to the bearing is likewise half of the load applied during
the tests. The exact load varies slightly from test to test and is thus not shown
here. The rationale for varying the load is explained in Section 5.2.4. In order
to get the estimated contact pressure Equations 4.37 to 4.40 are used based
on the initial estimate of (wz)max. The estimate is shown and compared with
other estimates in Section 7.1.5.

outer ring and the roller (j = O) or the inner ring and the roller (j = I). Rx,j
and Ry,j are used to determine a radius ratio αr,j (Equation 4.6), for both the
outer and inner contacts. Equations 4.47 to 4.50 show how the eﬀective radii
are calculated for the inner and outer rings.

d is the diameter of the rolling element, de is the pitch diameter, rI and rO are

The parameters are shown in Figure 4.4. Not all of the parameters are easily
obtained since they are not made public by the manufacture of the bearing.
Because of this, the parameters are approximated for this thesis.
Hamrock states that for a spherical roller the ratio between the crown radius
and the diameter of the roller is rr/d ≈4.
If the diameter of a roller is
measured fairly accurately, and the crown radius can be calculated.
The

circle segment the width and height are measured. Equation 4.51 show the

Rr,j = rj
2rr
(4.52)
Rr must be in the following range: 0.51 ≤Rr ≤0.54. It is most commonly
0.52 for ball bearings with conformity ratio for the outer race slightly larger
than the inner race.
In this case, the inner race radius is calculated from
Equation 4.52 by assuming a Rr of 0.53 which is slightly smaller than the one
calculated for the outer race: 0.54. Finally, the pitch diameter and the contact
angle is roughly estimated by measurement on a assembled bearing.
With all the parameters calculated the iterative process can be performed.
The calculations are shown in Appendix 8.1. The results of these calculations
are compared to a test performed on the test rig, including how large a load
it would take to achieve the same contact pressure as seen in the test. Furt-
hermore, a basic lifetime calculation for the bearing will be compared to the
lifetime seen in the test. These calculations will be presented in Section 4.3.2.
4.3.2
Basic Bearing Life
In addition calculating the load on the heaviest loaded roller, a simple lifetime
calculation is performed. The theory behind these calculations is from the
SKF Rolling Bearing Catalogue [9, p. 64]. The bearing data is likewise found
in the catalogue.

4.4
Test Speciﬁc Calculations
Before being able to perform the RCF testing for this thesis, some analytical
calculations had to be conducted to determine the operating parameters. The
theory needed to carry out these calculations has been introduced in the above
sections. In this section, how these calculations have been performed will be
presented along with references.
4.4.1
Cone Interference Fit
As stated in Section 3.2 the ﬁts between the test rings and the drive shafts
are cone interference ﬁts. As a test ring is pressed onto the shaft, it expands
causing large hoop stresses to develop in the ring creating a pressure build-up
==PAGE:59==
4.4. TEST SPECIFIC CALCULATIONS
39
between the test ring and the shaft, thus ﬁxing the two parts together.
To assess if the desired ﬁt pressure is achieved the diametric expansion is
measured. The method to relate the magnitude of expansion to the ﬁt pressure
presented in this section is formulated in the textbook "Machine Elements
Analysis and Design" [15, Sec. 5.3]. The surface area of the frustum on the
shaft must be known to determine the forces acting in the ﬁt. The surface
area of a frustum is calculated as shown in Equation 4.57.

4.4.2
Contact Pressure
To calculate the external force, F1, needed to achieve the desired contact
pressure the theory presented in Chapter 4 is used. The contact is assumed
to be rectangular, thus Dy ⇒∞. Both test rings are from the same bearings
and are thus considered to be made from the same material. The following
must be known to perform the calculations:
• Principal radii of curvature, rax and rbx
• Modulus of elasticity, E
• Poisson’s ratio, ν
• Contact width in the axial direction, ll
• Desired contact pressure, pmax
Because Dy is assumed inﬁnite only the eﬀective radius in the x-direction
is calculated using Equation 4.2. Equation 4.16 is then used to determine
the eﬀective modulus of elasticity. The radial load per unit length, w′
z, is
calculated using Equation 4.38.
From this, the dimensionless load can be
calculated using Equation 4.37. From Equation 4.40 the external load can now
==PAGE:64==
44
CHAPTER 4. ANALYTICAL CALCULATIONS
be isolated and converted to hydraulic pressure using the method presented
in Section 4.4.3.

[1] during his PhD work.

4.4.4
Slip
To apply slip in a test the slide-roll ratio (SRR) is used to determine how much
the surfaces are sliding in relation to each other. The SRR is calculated from
Equation 4.81 using the frequencies of each drive f1 and f2. This equation
was used by Janakiraman, Klit, Jensen, and Gronbaek [16].
SRR = 2
 
f2 −f1
f2 + f1
!
(4.81)
First, a given SRR and frequency of the master drive (Drive 2) is decided.
From this, the frequency of the slave drive (Drive 1) can be calculated. During
a test, each drive would be set to their given frequencies and the test would
be run until failure.
==PAGE:66==
==PAGE:67==
5 Test Execution
5.1
Performing the Tests
The practical and execution of tests are described in this chapter. The ﬁrst
test was performed to establish the operating parameters and procedures for
future tests. Because of this, there are some distinctions between Test 1 and
the other tests.
5.1.1
Preparing for a Test
At ﬁrst, the inner rings were to be removed from the donor bearings. Not
knowing how to best do this the outer ring and the roller cage on the ﬁrst
bearing was cut in half. This method proved to be very time consuming and
diﬃcult. The next bearing was disassembled by turning the inner ring and
rollers out of the bearing as observed in Figure 5.1 and breaking the roller
cage. The roller cage is thin and most likely made of a low carbon steel [9, p.
152], which made it possible to break the parts holding the rollers in place.
With some of the rollers removed, the inner ring was easy to remove. This
method proved to be easy and harmless to the inner ring and is thus used to
disassemble the remaining bearings.
47
==PAGE:68==
48
CHAPTER 5. TEST EXECUTION
Figure 5.1: Assembled bearing shown with rollers turned perpendicular to the outer
ring.
To avoid two opposing edges rolling against each other as seen in Figure 3.9b,
the contact surface of one ring was ground to a width of 4 mm in the axial
direction. The other ring was not ground and was thus left with a width in
the axial direction of 2 mm. Throughout the ﬁrst test ﬂuctuations in the force
and torque was observed and to reduce this, both rings were ground in future
tests. One ring was thus ground to 3 mm while the other was ground to 5 mm
to keep 1 mm between the edges on both sides of the contact. After grinding
both diameters to the contact surfaces were measured as well as the exact
width of the 3 mm ring. The surface roughness, Ra, of the ground surfaces
was also measured. However, due to the narrow width, reliable measurements
were hard to produce.
The rings were then pressed onto the shafts till a diametral expansion of 0.12
mm was measured. For the individual tests, the corresponding ﬁt pressure
has been calculated with Equation 4.77.
5.1.2
Operating the Test Rig
After assembling the test rig as described in Section 3.1, the rings were brought
into contact using the hydraulic pressure arrangement.
While moving the
shafts closer to each other Drive 2 was rotated slowly to determine the point
at which the two rings made contact. After the contact was established Drive
2 was turned oﬀagain. After this the following parameters were adjusted in
the LabVIEW program:
• "Intersect Y pressure scale" adjusted to get "Force between testdiscs [N]"
approximately 0 N.
==PAGE:69==
5.1. PERFORMING THE TESTS
49
• "Intersect Y Torque scale" adjusted to get "Torque on shaft (Drive 2)"
about 0 Nm.
• "sample length Torque" adjusted to 30.
• "sample length mean RPM Drive 1" changed to 30.
• "sample length mean RPM drive 2" changed to 30.
During the ﬁrst test, the last three parameters were not adjusted. This was
done for the remaining tests. In Figure 5.2 a screen shot of the monitoring
program is shown.
Figure 5.2: The program in LabVIEW which monitor the test rig and record data.
All the adjusted values, as well as readouts from the LabVIEW program, were
noted in a logbook. This logbook was used as a check list during operation
of the test rig and to keep track of operating parameters. An example of a
logbook is shown in Appendix 8.1.
The test was then started in the following order:
• "Pumpe olieﬁlter" started.
• "Pumpe smøreolie" started.
• "Pumpe oliekøler" started.
• "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" started simultaneously at 10.0 %
and 10.5 % respectively.
==PAGE:70==
50
CHAPTER 5. TEST EXECUTION
• Speed increased for "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" simultaneously
to 70.5 % and 70.3 % respectively, corresponding to approximately 2009
rpm.
• Log started.
• Contact pressure is increased slightly.
• The speed of "Motor drive 2" is adjusted for the desired torque.
• Contact pressure increased further till the desired force is reached.
For the ﬁrst test, contact pressure was increased before increasing rotational
speed. This procedure was changed for the remaining tests as it introduced
additional torque in the contact.
The method used to stop the test rig is shown below:
• Contact pressure was reduced till "Force between testdiscs [N]" was below
0.
• "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" stopped.
• Log stopped.
• "Pumpe smøreolie" stopped.
• "Pumpe oliekøler" stopped.
• "Pumpe olieﬁlter" stopped.
At ﬁrst, the rotational speed was lowered before reducing the contact pressure.
As with the start up procedure, this was changed during the ﬁrst test because
it introduced additional torque.
After stopping the test rig, the log was saved to a data ﬁle. The raw data ﬁles
are handed in digitally. The parameters recorded in the log are as follows:
1. Time [s]
2. Hydraulic pressure [Bar]
3. Torque [Nm]
4. Torque Mean [Nm]
5. RPM Drive 1
6. RPM mean Drive 1
==PAGE:71==
5.1. PERFORMING THE TESTS
51
7. RPM Drive 2
8. RPM mean Drive 2
9. Counter drive 1
10. Counter drive 2
Originally "Oil temperature (C°)" was recorded, but while performing Tests
1 to 7, the module connecting the sensor to LabVIEW was not available.
Since the oil mainly functions as a coolant for this present work the exact
temperature is not deemed necessary. It was noted during the ﬁrst tests that
the oil pipes leading from the drip tray to the oil reservoir only felt warm to
the touch meaning that a critical oil temperature was not reached. In Test
8 it was, however, possible to measure the oil temperature just before the oil
enters the contact. Because the tests are performed with approximately the
same parameters, the temperature is assumed to be roughly the same for all
tests.
5.1.3
After the Tests
When a signiﬁcant change in the operating noise was detected (a loud banging
noise) it indicated that some degree of pitting damage had occurred. The test
rig was stopped, and the test rings were inspected. An example of signiﬁcant
pitting damage on a test ring is shown in Figure 5.3.
Figure 5.3: Large pit developed on test ring 2-1 (5 mm ring).
The damage which is shown in Figure 5.3 can be categorised as a catastrophic
failure because a pit of this size would reduce the bearings ability to function
and quickly lead to further damage.
==PAGE:72==
52
CHAPTER 5. TEST EXECUTION
When pitting damage was observed the rings were removed from the test rig
and pressed oﬀthe shafts with a hydraulic press. When the damaged rings
were taken oﬀthe shafts, the surface roughness Ra was measured again avoi-
ding any large pits.
It was evaluated if any noteworthy change in surface
roughness had occurred in seemingly undamaged areas.
The test rings were then given to the project partners at DTU Risø for furt-
her studies. The characterization performed at DTU Risø was as mentioned
at the beginning of Chapter 3 3D X-ray tomography and scanning electron
microscopy. The X-ray tomography was carried out using a Zeiss Xradia 520
Versa. X-ray tomography is performed by directing a high energy beam at a
tungsten target which in turn ﬁres X-rays through the test piece onto a de-
tector screen. The X-rays are then processed, and a 3D map is reconstructed
using a Feldkamp algorithm [17]. Thus a picture can be constructed showing
the inside of the ring. Figure 5.4 shows the pit on ring 2-1 at an angle. Below
the pit, inside the material, a crack can be seen moving parallel to the surface.
Figure 5.4: Reconstruction of the pit observed in test ring 2-1 (5 mm ring) using
X-ray tomography.
The second characterization uses a Zeiss Supra 35 scanning electron microscope
to produce 2D images as shown in Figure 5.5. In electron microscopy, an elec-
tron microscope is focused onto the surface of the test piece. The beam moves
across the surface line by line, thus covering the entire surface. As the electron
beam hits the surface, electrons from the surface are broken oﬀ, leaving the
surface in diﬀerent directions. This scattering is used to produce a black and
white image that is shaded by the distribution of scattered electrons. The
electrons from the beam ricochet oﬀthe surface in approximately the same
directions as they came. The beam electrons are used to determine diﬀerent
material inclusions in the matrix material. Additionally, when the electrons
are broken oﬀfrom the atoms in the surface, X-rays are produced. These X-
rays can be used to determine the material composition of the test specimen.
==PAGE:73==
5.2. TEST SERIES
53
Figure 5.5: SEM image of the pit observed in test ring 2-1 (5 mm ring).
The SEM characterization is a destructive method, while the X-ray tomo-
graphy can be a nondestructive method. The Vickers hardness was also me-
asured both on the contact surface and along the mid-thickness in the cross-
section of the ring. The load applied during the Vickers test was 1 kg, with
a dwell time of 15 s. The Vickers hardness measures are presented in Section
7.1.6.
5.2
Test Series
In this section, brief descriptions of the individual tests will be presented. As
repeatedly stated throughout the thesis the goal of the tests was to investigate
slip.
However, due to problems producing a solid baseline, this was never
realised. The general operational parameters for the 8 tests performed are
shown in Table 5.1.

It is evident from Table 5.1 that mainly tests with 3.5 GPa contact pressure has
been performed. It was desired to perform tests at this high contact pressure
because pitting damage occurs relatively quickly. Tests 3 and 5 was conducted
to replicate Test 2 to establish a stable baseline for the coming tests. However
signiﬁcant deviations were observed in the lifetime of the three tests. Test 4
was performed to investigate the increase in the lifetime when reducing the
contact pressure. The increase in lifetime proved to be signiﬁcant, and the
contact pressure was thus further increased in Test 5. Test 6 was conducted
to replicate Test 4 to investigate if the large deviations in the lifetime were
due to the high contact pressure. However, Test 6 failed even faster than the
previous tests thus resulting in the largest deviation observed. When inves-
tigating the possible cause of these deviations, it was discovered that all test
specimens were slightly conical as a consequence of the grinding performed.
One ring (ring 8-1, 5 mm ring) was measured in the lab to determine the ex-
tent of the error. The measurement report is included in Appendix 8.1. From
these ﬁne measurements, it was determined that over an axial width of 4 mm
the diameters deviated 0.0616 mm corresponding to a cone angle of approxi-
mately 0.88°. Because only one ring was measured in the lab, the exact angles
of the previous rings are not known. The seventh and eighth ring pairs were
reground, and test rings for future tests have been ground with greater care.
This means that only Test 7 and 8 have been performed as originally intended.
Calculations performed before the tests were roughly identical for all the tests.
The most noteworthy diﬀerence between the ﬁrst three tests and the last ﬁve
was that the width of the contact was updated when calculating the external
force F1. Representable examples of the calculations for contact pressure and
ﬁt pressure are included in Appendix 8.1 and 8.1.

5.2.1
Test 1
The ﬁrst test was performed to establish the contact pressure level at which
to perform the coming tests. Another purpose was to gain experience with
operating the test rig. The contact pressure for the ﬁrst ring was intended to
be 1 GPa and the planned interference ﬁt pressure was 100 MPa. During the
test, a minor typing error was discovered in the contact pressure calculations,
resulting in an actual contact pressure of 1.42 GPa.
The actual interference ﬁt pressure was determined to be 136 MPa.
The
diﬀerence originated because the calculations initially performed called for a
diametric expansion of 0.12 mm at the ﬁt diameter. However, the outside
diameter was measured to obtain the diametric expansion. Performing new
calculations showed that if the desired interference ﬁt pressure should have
been established the diametric expansion of the outside diameter should have
been 0.089 mm. It was however chosen not to alter the ﬁt pressure for the
next tests, and a diametric expansion of 0.12 mm was intended for mounting
all test rings. The ﬁt pressure was chosen to avoid altering more than one
parameter for the next test and was further proven capable of keeping the
ring in position. The initial values for the test rings before the test are shown
in Table 5.2.

==PAGE:76==
56
CHAPTER 5. TEST EXECUTION
Figure 5.6: What appears to be a small crack observed on ring 1-2 (3 mm ring).
The results of the ﬁrst test are shown in Section 7.1. Some changes were made
before performing the next test:
• Both rings was to be ground.
1. Ring 2-1 contact area width 5 mm.
2. Ring 2-2 contact area width 3 mm.
• Contact pressure increased to 3.5 GPa
The rings were ground to lower the noise seen in the data, and the contact
pressure should be increased because no signiﬁcant damage was observed on
the test rings. The contact pressure of 3.5 GPa is based on calculations per-
formed by SKF for the permanent deformation of the heaviest loaded rolling
element. It is stated in the rolling bearing catalogue [9, p. 64], that a roller
bearing exposed to a pure radial load, resulting in a contact pressure of 4
GPa, will give a permanent deformation of the rolling element and raceway
of approximately 0.0001 the roller diameter. A contact pressure of 3.5 GPa
will thus result in a minimal permanent deformation if the load is applied
statically. As the load is applied dynamically, the contact surface should not
be permanently deformed, as it is not desired for the tests.
The grinding machine was renovated between Tests 1 and 2 meaning that a
more precise and reliable grinding operation could be expected.

In Table 5.4 it is noticed that the interference ﬁt pressure is very sensitive to
small changes in the diametric expansion. The reason limiting the expansion
of ring 3-1 is that the rings tend to "jump" when they are pressed onto the
shaft. As when the static friction is overcome to move the ring, the dyna-
mic friction is not great enough to hold back the ring and make the motion
smooth, thus movement of the rings happens in steps, and the target is easily
missed.

5.3
Data Treatment
The post-test data treatment is primarily performed in Matlab® R2016a
though other programs such as Maple™2016 and Microsoft® Excel 2007 are
used. During the data treatment in Matlab® build-in functions are mainly
used to perform trivial calculations such as calculating averages.
When starting and especially when stopping the test rig, some data is produ-
ced with pressures below zero. This data is removed from the data ﬁles before
further data treatment. The data would also have an adverse eﬀect on the
average value calculated as it would be artiﬁcially lowered.
For Tests 1 to 7 no data "Oil temperature (C°)" was recorded and is therefore
manually removed from these data ﬁles.
The decimal separator is changed from "," to "." to make the data ﬁles com-
patible with Matlab® using the script Decimal_changer.m (Appendix 8.1).
The data ﬁles related to a speciﬁc test are then imported into a raw database
ﬁle. The Matlab® script FileImportToDbraw.m created for this operation is
shown in Appendix 8.1. This script prompts the user to enter the number of
ﬁles to be included in the database and prompts for the speciﬁc ﬁles wanted.
Cell arrays are created for each parameter included the log ﬁle, and the cor-
responding data is saved into these cells.
The database ﬁle is imported into a new script in which individual data tre-
atment for each test is performed. The script DataTreatmentRingPair8.m
related to Test 8 is shown in Appendix 8.1, the other scripts are not inclu-
ded as they are roughly identical to DataTreatmentRingPair8.m. The script
creates a summary of the load history the test ring has experienced. This
load history is presented in a table and plots for contact pressure, torque and
speed during the whole test. The script ﬁrst creates a time vector which is
used to plot the data. Next, the total number of revolutions is calculated. The
number in the raw data is the total number of signals received. However, on
one revolution two signals are recorded, thus this number is divided by 2. The
average and moving average of the torque is calculated and plotted against
the total time. In the plots of the ﬁrst test, the raw data was included to show
the noise in the data. The raw data is not presented for any other tests as it
==PAGE:87==
5.3. DATA TREATMENT
67
disrupts the plot clarity. In the raw data, pressure in the hydraulic cylinder
is recorded. To calculate the force applied to the test rings during the test
Equation 4.80 is used. The force is then used to derive the contact pressure
which has been between the test rings during the test. To do this Equations
4.37, 4.38 and 4.40 was used. From the resulting contact pressure vector, the
average and moving average could be calculated for the plot. A target range is
created to indicate the level at which it is desired to have the contact pressure.
For Tests 1 to 7 the ﬁnal calculation performed was the calculations of the
rotational speeds of each drive as well as the average and moving average.
However, for test 8 the temperature is, as mentioned earlier, also included.
The temperature is extracted from the raw data, and a plot showing the
average and moving average is created.
5.3.1
Weibull Probability Plot
To better interpret the data generated from the tests a Weibull probability
plot is created. The Weibull probability plot will be used to relate the number
of revolutions to the likelihood of failure for a given set of load parameters.
When a ﬁt is created for the ﬁrst set of parameters another ﬁt can be created.
In the second ﬁt, only one load parameter should be changed. If the lifetime is
aﬀected by the changed parameter diﬀerence in the likelihood of failure would
be observed.
The distribution used is the two-parameter Weibull distribution shown in
Equation 5.1.
f (rev) = 1 −exp −
 
rev
η
!θ
(5.1)
rev is the variable representing revolutions, η is the value of rev at which 63.2%
has failed, and θ is the slope of the ﬁtted curve representing the variability of
lifetime. To establish the x- and y-axis of the plot Equation 5.1 is linearized
which gives the following expressions for the axes.
y = ln

ln
 
1
1 −f (rev)
!

(5.2)
x = ln (rev)
(5.3)
Which can be used to express Equation 5.1 as follows:
y = θx −θ ln (η)
(5.4)
The basic form of the axes is established this enables the creation of a curve
from the failure data. To do this the Matlab® function wblplot is used.
==PAGE:88==
68
CHAPTER 5. TEST EXECUTION
This function plots the data and adds the best ﬁt curve to the plot. In order
to get the η and θ parameters the function wblfit is used. If the likelihood of
failure is desired for a speciﬁc number of revolutions the function wblcdf can
be used. The function simply calculates Equation 5.1 with given values for
η and θ [18]. The Weibull probability plot created for this thesis is shown in
Section 7.1 and the script FinalDataTreatement.m in which the calculations
were performed is included in Appendix 8.1.
==PAGE:89==
6 Numerical Calculations
6.1
Introduction to COMSOL Multiphysics
COMSOL Multiphysics® is a numerical simulation environment which can
simulate a wide range of physical phenomena using ﬁnite element principals
as well as equation based modelling approaches. COMSOL is thus able to
calculate solids as well as ﬂuids and combine the diﬀerent environments into
a single model. For this thesis, the area of interest is the contact between two
solids. Modelling two separate objects contacting is not easy as the common
ﬁnite element method usually deals with forces applied directly to a node in
the model. COMSOL is thus to be used as it has build in functions to deal
with the contact of two surfaces [19].
6.1.1
Contact Modelling in COMSOL
To perform the contact modelling in COMSOL the book "Introduction to
COMSOL Multiphysics" [20] has been used as a guideline to create the geo-
metry. The example "Cylinder Roller Contact" (Appendix 8.1) has been used
to set up the model. The procedure from the example is reviewed in Section
6.2.1.
The following is based on the documentation included in the program. A user
guide created for contact modelling has been used to troubleshoot the model.
1.
When performing a contact analysis, the model solves two separate solutions;
the contact state and the contact force. If friction is not included the contact
state is either in contact or not. The force is the normal contact pressure in
the model. If friction is included the contact state will be: sticking or sliding.
The tangential force is added as a force vector. The special steps performed
when setting up a contact model are:
• Create contact pair.
1File path: File −Help −Documentation −Structural Mechanics Module −Users
Guide −Structural Mechanics Modeling −Contact Modeling
69
==PAGE:90==
70
CHAPTER 6. NUMERICAL CALCULATIONS
• Create Contact nodes.
• Form an assembly.
The contact pair can either be created automatically when forming the as-
sembly or done manually. The contact pair consists of two surfaces which
are intended to have contact. These are called the source and the destina-
tion boundaries. The destination boundary is coupled to the source boundary
meaning that "the destination receives its displacement from the source". If
two parts in contact have equal stiﬀness, the geometry is used to determine
the source and destination boundaries. The concave boundary must be the
source and the convex the destination. For the pair to function the mesh of
the destination must always be at least two times ﬁner than for the source.
The contact node is added to provide the correct settings for the model from
the contact pair just created. The form assembly assembles the diﬀerent geo-
metry parts which are in contact into objects.
As the shear stresses should be modelled in the more advanced models, friction
must be included in these models. The inclusion of friction depends on the
contact algorithm used for solving the model. The default algorithm is the
augmented Lagrangian method. The other possibility is the penalty method.
The augmented Lagrangian method is the default as it provides better accu-
racy even though it has a higher computational demand, require additional
degrees of freedom and is less stable. If the model does converge the algo-
rithm will ensure that there is no penetration between the contacting objects.
Contact pressure and friction are added as degrees of freedom. The penalty
method is, on the other hand, simple and robust. If this algorithm is used, the
method for calculating friction force can be chosen independently of the nor-
mal direction pressure. The penalty method works by inserting a stiﬀspring
between the object. The spring only works in compression. By doing this, the
instability from the augmented Lagrangian method is avoided.
6.2
Models
In this section, the diﬀerent models will be elaborated. The example model
and the ﬁrst model being the only models to be performed.
The concept
behind Models 2 and 3 will brieﬂy be outlined.
6.2.1
Example Model
The example is based on an inﬁnitely long cylinder which is placed on a solid
block. In the example, the cylinder is made from steel, and the block is made
from aluminium. However, when performing the example for this thesis, the
bearing material introduced in Section 3.3.1 has been used. The object of
==PAGE:91==
6.2. MODELS
71
the example is to determine the contact pressure distribution from the centre
of contact to the edge of the contact. An analytical solution is included in
the example for easy comparison with the numerical result. The equation for
contact pressure is given as:
p =
