p is contact pressure, w′
z is the load per unit length, E′ is the eﬀective modu-
lus of elasticity, Rx is the eﬀective radius of curvature and x is the coordinate
on the x-axis. This analytical solution for the contact pressure resembles the
equations from Hamrock et al. [12] quite well. It is however only a function of
the position in the x-direction and gives a slightly diﬀerent maximum pressure
when compared to the Equation 4.40.
The model is based on the contact problem being in plain strain. This faci-
litates a 2D model. The problem is further reduced by utilising the vertical
symmetry axis. The model geometry is shown as the blue framed section in
Figure 6.1. A point load is applied to the top of the cylinder which forces the
cylinder into the block below. To compare the example to a test, it is chosen
to use the external force from Test 7 as well as the geometry of test ring 7-2.
This means that the external force F1 is used as the point load in the model
with the magnitude 21078.12 N.
Figure 6.1: The geometry in 3D and the corresponding 2D model. Figure 1 from
the COMSOL Roller example.
==PAGE:92==
72
CHAPTER 6. NUMERICAL CALCULATIONS
In this model, it is necessary to assume an initial contact pressure to make the
model stable, because the cylinder, in its initial position, is free to move in the
vertical direction which makes the conﬁguration singular. An alternative to
assuming the initial contact pressure would be to either overlap the surfaces
from the beginning or to support the cylinder with a weak spring. To assume
the initial contact pressure, the external force is divided by an assumed ﬁnal
contact area. While setting up the model this contact area proved to be very
sensitive, and while the half width of the test ring contact surface was used
(3.343/2 mm), the length could only be 7 or 8 mm. To minimise the size of
the area a 7 mm length was chosen. A beneﬁt of assuming an initial contact
pressure is that the model will likely converge faster.
The mesh in the model is reﬁned close to the contact because the contact area
is small. The reﬁnement is in the cylindrical domain performed with a free
triangular mesh and in the block domains a mapped mesh.
The solver used for the model is a build-in stationary segregated solver. The
solver is used with its default setup and solves the displacement and the con-
tact pressure separately.
To create the model the step-by-step guide from the example was followed.
Because of this, all the steps will not be elaborated in great detail.
The
geometry created can be seen in Figure 6.2. The parameter d as been changed
to H and W in the model performed. H and W are used in the same way as
d would have been, but their size is increased to accommodate the larger ring.
Figure 6.2: The 2D geometry created in COMSOL.
The geometry domains (Figure 6.2) has been united and converted to a solid
using the command Convert to Solid and assembled using the command
Form Union/Assembly. This is always the last command in the geometry
==PAGE:93==
6.2. MODELS
73
section and helps decide how the program perceive the geometry. Next, the
contact pair between the cylinder and the block is deﬁned. The surfaces used
are the two surfaces that are going to be pushed together. The boundary
conditions and the load are applied next. Only half the load deﬁned earlier
is used as the model is only half the width. In the settings for the contact
boundary, the initial contact pressure is deﬁned as (F/2)/(lc · th).
The mesh of the cylinder is created as a free triangular mesh as stated above.
The mesh size is deﬁned from the edge which is going to be in contact with the
block. The maximum element size is set to 0.6 mm on the contacting edge.
Figure 6.3 shows the mesh for the cylinder. The blue edge in the bottom will
be contacting the block and is the controlling edge for the mesh.
Figure 6.3: The mesh for the cylinder.
The mesh for the block is made in two stages. To get the top half meshed pro-
perly the blue edges shown in Figure 6.4 are chosen. The number of elements
on each of these edges is set to 20. The bottom square shown in Figure 6.2
may have a very course mesh as this domain is not interesting for the model.
Only 10 elements are chosen along the side length of this square.
Figure 6.4: The mesh for the block.
After meshing the solver was set up. Only one parameter was changed: a
scale factor was set to 109. The model was calculated two times: ﬁrst with
the point load in the top of the cylinder and second with the point load in the
==PAGE:94==
74
CHAPTER 6. NUMERICAL CALCULATIONS
middle of the cylinder, to investigate the inﬂuence of the loading position on
the result. The plots generated from this model is shown in Section 7.1.7.
6.2.2
Model 1
Model 1 was created based on the example model described in the previous
section. The symmetry condition was however not used for this model because
the idea was to create a baseline for later models in which the cylinder was
able to rotate and create slip in the contact. The geometry created for Model
1 is shown in Figure 6.5.
Figure 6.5: The geometry of Model 1.
The block area directly beneath the cylinder is made larger than in the exam-
ple to facilitate a larger area with a ﬁne mesh. The cylinder itself is also made
into two separate domains. This was done to ﬁnd the maximum stress within
the domain. As the symmetry condition was not present in this model, the
cylinder was able to move in all directions at ﬁrst. This caused it to role oﬀ
to one side or the other when it was pressed into the block. The side to which
it rolled depended on the mesh of the solids. Some points on a mesh are slig-
htly harder than others, and the cylinder would thus roll to the side at which
deformation happened easiest. To ﬁx this problem a prescribed displacement
of 0 m in the x-direction was created. This constraint the cylinder to only
move in the vertical direction. A point load is placed in the centre point in
the cylinder as this resembles the loading in the RCF test. In the beginning,
the model had many issues with convergence. The initial contact pressure has
thus been set to a ﬁxed value of 600 MPa.
When meshing the model the same procedure as in the example was utilised.
For the free triangular mesh in the cylinder, the maximum element size was
==PAGE:95==
6.2. MODELS
75
prescribed to 4.5 mm, and the minimum was set to 0.004 mm. This relatively
high maximum element size originate from a failed attempt to perform a mesh
convergence study. The idea was to use a parametric sweep to gradually reﬁne
the mesh until convergence was found with respect to the maximum von Mises
stress at a given point below the contact. A lot of time was devoted to making
the convergence work, but due to multiple failures in diﬀerent stages of the
model this never succeed. The failures likely originated because the diﬀerent
meshes did not match well enough.
The meshed basic model is shown in
Figures 6.6 and 6.7
Figure 6.6: The cylinder meshed. The blue edges in the bottom controls the mesh
reﬁnement.
Figure 6.7: Mesh of the top half of the block.
In Figure 6.7 the number of elements to divide each of the diﬀerent edges had
to match. The blue edges thus have 35 elements, and the bottom edge of the
large square has 15 elements. The lowest square is also in this model meshed
very course and is therefore not included.
==PAGE:96==
76
CHAPTER 6. NUMERICAL CALCULATIONS
The model has been calculated in the state as shown, with a point load of
21078.12 N and a thickness of 3.343/2 mm. The results of this is shown in
Section 7.1.7
A possible reason why the parametric sweep did not work is that the mesh
of the source boundary (the block) was too ﬁne in comparison to the mesh of
the destination boundary (the cylinder). However, this does not explain why
the model seems to function with the coarsest mesh of the cylinder. Due to
the amount of time invested in the parametric sweep no further models were
created.
6.2.3
Model 2
The object of Model 2 was to have a solid shaft in the centre with a ring around
it. The force was to be added to the centre of the shaft which should transfer
the load onto the ring via a contact pair. The ring should then be pressed
into the block as described previously. Between the ring and the shaft, a ﬁt
pressure would be simulated by using thermal expansion of the shaft. The
ﬁt pressure would thus create hoop stresses in the ring which would better
simulate the actual situation.
6.2.4
Model 3
Model 3 should build upon Model 2 and introduce rotation to the shaft and
ring thus creating slip in the contact. The last step would be to replace the
block with another rotating shaft and ring to be able to simulate the actual
situation.
==PAGE:97==
7 Results and Discussion
The object of this chapter is to present the data gathered during the tests, as
well as discuss the results.
7.1
Results
In Table 7.1 the lifetimes of each test are shown along with the load and speeds
of the individual tests. The deviation in lifetime and the recorded number of
revolutions between the tests are easily noticed when compared. When com-
paring the lifetimes of the individual tests to their mean contact pressure and
torque a clear link is not identiﬁed. For instance, when Test 7 and Test 8 are
compared the shortest lifetime is recorded for Test 8 even though the average
contact pressure and average torque are lower than recorded for Test 7. Both
tests are, as stated earlier, performed after regrinding the test rings. The life
of Test 8 is inﬂuenced by prematurely stopping the test as stated in Section
5.2.8, but when compared Test 8 seems more damaged illustrated 5.12b. With
such advanced damage on test ring 8-2, it seems unlikely that Test 8 would
be able to reach the same lifetime as Test 7. As Test 8, according to the
recorded values in Table 7.1, have not been loaded as much as Test 7 another
factor must cause the advanced damage. In Section 5.2.8 the roundness was
mentioned as a possible cause of extra damage. This is likely; however, it is
not possible to draw any conclusions from these two tests alone.
Drive 1 has a greater number of revolutions for each test except in Test 4.
The diﬀerence is caused by the small variation in outer diameter of the rings.
The variation is due to the Test 4 rings being switched around as they were
mounted on the shafts. When starting the test rig for Test 4, the diﬀerence
was noticed. It was deemed unnecessary to change as the test was already
started at this point and the only diﬀerence when switching the test rings
would be which drive was fastest.
The surface roughness measurements are not complete which is evident from
Table 7.1. The measurements were not reliable as the measured surface was
only 5 mm wide, and the measuring devise normally need minimum 8 mm
77
==PAGE:98==
78
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
ﬂat surface to perform a good measurement. Reliable measurements were not
possible to perform on the narrow ring. Consequently, mainly measurements
for the wide ring are included. The measurement was never performed on the
test rings from Test 6.

To better visualise the spread of the 8 lifetimes the scatter plot shown in
Figure 7.1 has been created. The plots show the total number of revolutions
for each test. To create the plot the mean number of revolutions for Drives 1
and 2 for each test was calculated from the values listed in Table 7.1.
Figure 7.1: Scatter plot showing the average number of revolutions for each test.
Figure 7.1 show that a longer lifetime can be expected when performing the
test with lower contact pressure (Test 1 and Test 4). However, Test 6 which
should have approximately the same lifetime as Test 4 does not ﬁt. The diﬀe-
rence is related to the misaligned grinding as stated earlier. Figure 7.1 shows
that a scatter plot of this type cannot be used to draw any conclusions. There-
fore, a Weibull probability plot was created for the tests having approximately
==PAGE:99==
7.1. RESULTS
79
3.5 GPa contact pressure. The theory behind performing the plot is explained
in Section 5.3.1. As not enough data points were obtained in this plot and the
tests with misalignment are plotted as well the Weibull probability plot is not
as reliable as was originally intended. The Weibull probability plot shown in
Figure 7.2 can, however, be used to show the principle of the Weibull plot.
Figure 7.2: Weibull probability plot created for the tests intended to be performed
at 3.5 GPa contact pressure.
In Figure 7.2 blue plus symbols show the data points to be ﬁtted. The red
best-ﬁt line has a solid section and two dashed sections. The solid part of the
line represents the data from the 25th percentile to the 75th percentile. From
the analysis the Weibull scale parameter η and the slope of the best-ﬁt line θ
are as follows:
• η = 2, 040, 086.68 rev
• θ = 5.11
63.2 % of tests performed at 3.5 GPa contact pressure should have failed at
approximately two million revolutions according to η. The slope of the line,
in this case, shows that the rate of failure will increase for increasing number
of revolutions. θ = 5.11 indicates a low variability in the lifetime but does not
indicate a high or a low lifetime. If the slope had been below 1 it would have
indicated a high rate of early failures caused by a diﬀerent mechanism than
fatigue or wear. The deviation between the ﬁtted line and the data points
illustrated in Figure 7.2 can be caused by one or more of the following:
• Diﬀerent failure mechanisms.
==PAGE:100==
80
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
• Diﬀerent operating environments.
• Diﬀerent manufacturer.
• Defects in the product.
The most likely causes for the deviations in Figure 7.2 are a mixture of dif-
ferent types of failures and diﬀerence in operating environments Lien [21].
The misalignment of the contacting surfaces has caused the contact pressure
to deviate signiﬁcantly. As a result, diﬀerent types of failures are observed.
The most common is the formation of a large pit in one location. The se-
cond failure type is the formation of many small pits along the contact line.
The second failure type is assumed to be the one which is desired as this
show even fatigue over the whole circumference. This failure type is also ob-
served in Test 7 and 8 which are performed closer to the intended line contact.
According to Brändlein et al. [8] Weibull probability plots based on test series
in the range of ﬁve to ten tests gives unreliable values for η and θ. For compa-
rison the basic lifetime calculations for bearings, L10, are based on θ = 27/20.
The large diﬀerence between θ for L10 and θ calculated for this work proves
that the results are uncertain. Further more that tests conducted with higher
stresses to produce faster results easily gives misjudgements. However as the
object of performing the Weibull probability plot (as stated in Section 5.3.1)
was to investigate the diﬀerence between two Weibull lines with one diﬀerent
loading parameter, the predicted η and θ are of less importance.
In the following sections, only a few selected plots are shown for the diﬀerent
measured parameters as the plots are somewhat similar. The plots from the
not included tests are shown in Appendix 8.1. The plots of Test 1 are included
to demonstrate the diﬀerence in running the test rig with and without expe-
rience and how much this impacts the results gained. The plots from Test 4
are included to show that roughly the same level of control was exerted for
the longest running test to the other tests. Finally, Tests 7 and 8 are shown
as they represent the test run with the new ground test rings.
7.1.1
Contact Pressure
In this section, four plots are included showing the development of the contact
pressure during Test 1, 4, 7 and 8. In each plot, a target line is shown with a
±50 N quantiles. The ±50 N range was based on the control over the contact
pressure achieved at the end of Test 1. The goal was to stay within this range
in the main part of each test. The average and moving average calculated
over the past 5 minutes are similarly shown in each plot. In Figure 7.3 the
target line is set in an odd position compared to the other tests. As explained
in Section 5.2.1 this is due to the calculation error originally made. This does
==PAGE:101==
7.1. RESULTS
81
however not aﬀect the quality of this speciﬁc test because the goal was to
investigate how the rings would be aﬀected in the test rig. Fluctuations in the
contact pressure could have produced a failure sooner which meant that the
contact pressure was suﬃcient. The periodical steep increases and decreases
are due to the start and stop of the test rig. In all other tests than Test 1 this
causes the average value to be drawn below the target line. In Test 1 the low
contact pressure meant that the initial heat up of the test rings would cause
the pressure to increase above the target pressure due to thermal expansion.
The hydraulic loading arrangement is sensitive to low pressures meaning that
the eﬀect of the heat up was hard to control. Because of this, it was only at
the end of the test that a satisfactory level of control was achieved. The raw
data is included in Figure 7.3 to demonstrate how well the moving average
represents the actual data but also to show that the raw data contains a great
deal of noise confusing the interpretation of the plot if included for the coming
tests.
Figure 7.3: Contact pressure measured during Test 1. The plot includes raw data.
In Test 4 (Figure 7.4) far better control of the contact pressure is achieved,
partly because the experience with the test rig made it possible to anticipate
when to increase the pressure.
When comparing the pressure range from
Figure 7.3 to the pressure range from Figure 7.4 a ±50 N range equals a
narrower pressure range for higher contact pressures, since Equation 4.40 on
calculating the contact pressure is a square root function of the increasing
external force. The eﬀect of this is that the contact pressure becomes easier
to control for higher values of applied force. The average pressure value in
Figure 7.4 is lower than the target pressure due to starting and stopping the
test rig.
==PAGE:102==
82
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
Figure 7.4: Contact pressure recorded from Test 4.
When comparing the moving averages of the test in Figures 7.4, 7.5 and 7.6
Test 4 seems to have a more ﬂuctuating course. However, this is due to the
scaling of the x-axis as Test 4 lasted signiﬁcantly longer.
Figure 7.5: Contact pressure recorded from Test 7.
After regrinding, the rings Test 7 and 8 should have been identical however
a miscommunication caused a wrong contact width to be used in the initial
calculations signiﬁcantly shifting the average and moving average from the
target force as shown in Figure 7.6.
Unfortunate the contact pressure de-
viation between the two has become signiﬁcantly larger than the deviation
observed between Test 5 and 7. Test 7 and 8 is therefore not performed at
the same contact pressure.
An eﬀect of performing the entire test in one continuous run is evident in

7.1.2
Torque Measured
In this section, the development of torque is shown in four plots from tests 1,
4, 7 and 8. Raw data is included in the ﬁrst plot to show the level of noise
experienced during the test. The goal for all the tests has been to apply as
low a torque as possible to simulate pure rolling. In Figure 7.7 a high torque
is observed in the beginning of Test 1. The high torque was applied to slowly
dial the torque level down to avoid damaging the master motor. At the end of
the test, an appropriate frequency for each drive was achieved, and the torque
measured only deviated ±2 Nm. The moving average is roughly zero for the
last part of the test whereas the average is slightly higher because of the high
torque at the beginning of the test.
==PAGE:104==
84
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION

average it is obvious that the sum torque experienced by the rings is roughly
zero. In Figure 7.8 the ﬂuctuations of the moving average seem sudden com-
pared to Figure 7.7. As with the plot showing the contact pressure for Test
4 the suddenness is due to the length of the test. The magnitude of the de-
viation between the moving average and the average is not high compared to
the noise ﬂuctuation level in the raw data. The abrupt changes in the moving
average occur when starting and stopping the test rig.
Figure 7.8: Torque measured throughout Test 4.

shown in Figures 7.9 and 7.10, indicating that Test 4 was controlled slightly
better than Test 7 and 8.

the high spot in the moving average. These very high values are caused by
starting the test rig. Whereas the location of the breakdown only has a peak
value of 6 Nm. Equally high values are not found for Test 8. The highest
value measured in the raw data for Test 8 is 6.4 Nm. A few cases of high slip
does thus not seem to aﬀect the lifetime in these tests as Test 8 failed 4 hours

As shown in Table 7.3 the bearing does not appear to be heavily loaded. With
an expected lifetime of about 7600 hours, the bearing would last roughly 5
years assuming an 8 hour work day with 200 work days a year. This seems
fairly normal for a bearing in an industrial machine. The test load would thus
not be suﬃcient to produce failure in a bearing within the time frame of this
thesis. The contact pressure is the maximum contact pressure which is loca-
ted between the roller and the inner ring because the contact is convex which
gives less contact area and higher pressure. The maximum contact pressure is
roughly the same as seen during Test 1. When considering Test 1, at 40 hours
of operation no signiﬁcant wear had occurred. The maximum deﬂection is the
sum of compression of the roller. The contact semi-width is related to the in-
ner ring contact. Both of these are considerably lower than was seen for Test 7.
When comparing the initial values with the converged values no change has
occurred in lifetime between the two. This is because the L10 calculations
only take force and speed as well as predetermined constants into considera-
tion. The contact pressure is however aﬀected by an increase of 12.88% which
is a relatively large error. This also proves that initial estimate made with
Equation 4.42 is not very accurate when performing calculations on a roller
bearing which could be expected as the equation is derived for a ball bearing.
If the bearing calculations are performed based on the same contact pressure
as seen in Test 7 the values shown in Table 7.4 are found. Here no iterative
calculations are needed.

Table 7.4 show that the lifetime of a bearing is signiﬁcantly decreased when
the contact pressure matches that of Test 7. If calculating the adjusted li-
fetime or the SKF lifetime the L10 value would probably increase. However,
considering how the lubrication is applied in the test rig, it is not possible to
say speciﬁcally how contaminated the oil in the contact is. It is thus not pos-
sible to give an exact estimate of these lifetimes and the L10 lifetime is used.
The L10 lifetime matches the lifetime seen in Test 7, means that it would be
possible to produce similar results by using a roller bearing and loading it
with an external force as shown in Table 7.4.
The maximum deﬂection and the contact semi-width do not compare well
with Test 7. This is possibly because to the theory is based on simpliﬁcations
and because the geometry of the contacts is slightly diﬀerent.
7.1.6
Symposium Paper
The ﬁrst test completed with high contact pressure was test 2 and these test
rings were thus the ﬁrst to be handed to the project partners at DTU Risø.
Test 2 became subject for a symposium paper written in cooperation with the
project partners at DTU Risø. The symposium paper is included in Appendix
8.1. The paper brieﬂy introduces the problems with premature bearing failure
seen in wind turbines thus establishing the relevance of the test.
Plots showing the contact pressure, the torque measured and the rotational
speeds of each drive are shown in the paper. The plots are included in Ap-
pendix 8.1. The plots illustrate that the contact pressure during the test was
approximate 3.5 GPa and the torque was acceptably low.
From the characterization performed at DTU Risø, the Vickers hardness was
measured. The hardness was 800 HV1 on the surface which corresponds to
64 HRC. Along the ring mid-thickness, the hardness was 740 HV1 which cor-
==PAGE:111==
7.1. RESULTS
91
responds to approximately 61 - 62 HRC. Figure 2.7 illustrate the relationship
between the hardness scales. Compared to the values given by SKF which was
presented in Section 3.3.1 it is evident that the core material has hardened
slightly while the outer surface has hardened 2 - 3 HRC. The contact surface
was thus hardened considerably during the test.
The X-ray tomography images of test ring 2-1 presented in the paper are shown
in Figure 7.14. In the ﬁgure, a 35 µm circumferential groove is observed near
the edge of the contact surface. The groove is marked with an arrow. In
Figure 7.14 the sub ﬁgure (a) is an area with no pits and is used to show that
the groove is present in the whole circumference. (b) and (c) show the same
pit at diﬀerent zooms to give a view of the groove and the pit respectively.
The groove is caused by the misalignment of the rings from the grinding
process and shows that a signiﬁcantly smaller contact width has been present
than originally planed. The pit is largest close to the groove and is therefore
assumed to have propagated from this location. The pit is approximatly 3
mm wide and 420 µm deep.
Figure 7.14: X-ray tomography image of test ring 2-1 shown in diﬀerent zooms.
The pit shown in Figure 7.14 (b) and (c) has also been investigated by SEM
(Figure 5.5). The ﬁgure shows the central portion of the coarse pit, where
multiple cracks are seen both near the pit and on the pit surface. However,
pit development has also been found in seemingly pit-free surfaces. Figure
7.15 shows an X-shape crack with arms extending over at least 0.5 mm, ob-
served near the circumferential groove marked in Figure 7.14 (a). This crack
is apparently very shallow and thus not observable in the X-ray tomography
image. Such cracks are considered to be precursors of pits. The defects ob-
==PAGE:112==
92
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
served on the contact surface appear to be examples of damage associated
with classical pitting failure. It is suggested that the cracks started below the
surface, where the shear stress had its maximum value.
Figure 7.15: X-shaped crack near the groove in Figure 7.14 (c) shown via a SEM
image.
The symposium paper is written after the discovery of the poorly performed
grinding. In the paper, it is thus concluded that the failure seen in Test 2 must
originate from the edge where the largest contact pressure is experienced. It
is also concluded that severe vibrations are likely in wind turbine applications
where manufacturing defects and misalignments can lead to premature failures
of the roller bearings in gear boxes.

The results obtained from COMSOL is presented. Because the models were
never properly ﬁnished only some basic plots are shown. Originally it was
desired to export the following values and compare them to the experimental
values:
• The depth of the maximum shear force.
• The maximum shear force.
• The maximum contact pressure.
These values were never exported due to the fact that the models was not
ﬁnished.

Figure 7.16: V. Mises stress example model.
Figures 7.16a and 7.16b show the diﬀerence of having the point load applied
on the top or in the center of the cylinder. When applying the load on the
top, compressive stresses are introduced at a point of the geometry which in
the RCF test is not in compression. As a result of this, the cylinder is slightly
deformed in the top, and the stresses are slightly smaller in the actual contact.
When the point load is moved to the centre the stresses in the cylinder just
above the contact is propagating further into the material.
Figures 7.17a and 7.17b the contact pressure distributions calculated from
the example model is shown. Both computed graphs are roughly identical,
the only diﬀerence between the two is that the maximum contact pressure is
==PAGE:114==
94
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
slightly higher when the point load is applied in the centre of the cylinder. The
slightly higher contact pressure also implies that the contact area is slightly
wider. However, at this load level, the diﬀerence is almost negligible.
(a) Contact pressure distributions; computed and analytical. Point load appiled on
top of the cylinder.
(b) Contact pressure distributions; computed and analytical. Point load applied in
the center of the cylinder.
Figure 7.17: Contact pressure distributions from the example model.
Model 1
When comparing the von Mises stresses from the example model to the von
Mises stresses shown in Figure 7.18 it is evident that the stresses in Model
1 are signiﬁcantly lower. A possible reason is that the mesh in Model 1 is
too coarse in the cylinder to properly calculate the solution. Another factor
is the assumed thickness of the model which is equal to the thickness in the
example model. The thickness has a great impact on both the contact pressure
==PAGE:115==
7.2. DISCUSSION
95
and the stresses in the model. If the thickness were increased in Model 1, it
would only further decrease the stresses and the contact pressure, which would
further diﬀerentiate the model from the example model. In Figure 7.19 the
contact pressure is shown. The lower contact pressure is as well caused by the
mesh being too coarse. Reﬁning the mesh would also smooth the graph more,
making it resemble the graphs in Figures 7.17a and 7.17b.
Figure 7.18: V. Mises stress in the deformed geometry in Model 1.
Figure 7.19: Contact pressure distributions in Model 1.
7.2
Discussion
The results gathered throughout the diﬀerent stages of this thesis are not
unambiguous, and because of this, it is hard to draw any speciﬁc conclusions
from them. This is evident from the diﬀerent lifetimes shown in Table 7.1
and Figure 7.1. Scatter was to be expected as this is normal for lifetime tests
on bearings as shown in Brändlein et al. [8, p.154].
The scatter shown in
==PAGE:116==
96
CHAPTER 7. RESULTS AND DISCUSSION
this thesis is however based on diﬀerences between each test instead of the
possible diﬀerences in the test pieces. The largest cause of inconsistencies be-
tween the tests, as repeatedly stated throughout this thesis, is undoubtedly
the improper grinding of the ﬁrst six test rings. The resulting misalignment
has caused the line contact length to vary, possibly with multiple millimetres.
To put this into perspective if the line contact length of Test 6 varied with
−1 mm the contact pressure would increase from 3.00 GPa to 3.62 GPa. This
kind of variation could easily result in plastic deformation of the test pieces
thus removing the basis for comparison of the tests. The parallelism of the
ground surface to the centre axis of the ring should have been inspected ear-
lier during the eﬀort to ﬁnd the source of deviation as the ground surface is
critical to the test. The inspection was however not performed earlier because
a high level of parallelism between the two is expected therefore a deviation
causing a cone angle of 0.88° is not expected when ordering a cylindrical grind.
Other factors have also inﬂuenced the quality of the test results. When moun-
ting the rings onto the shafts the centre axes of the shafts and rings should
coincide. The cone ﬁt should facilitate proper alignment of the axes however
this might not be the case for every ring mounted. When comparing Test 7
and 8 a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in roundness is observed. This could indicate
poor alignment of the centre axes which would result in an increased vibra-
tion level. In addition incorrect measurements are believed to have aﬀected
the tests. As stated above, the line contact length must be very accurate to
perform a test with the correct contact pressure. If the expansion of the out-
side diameters of the test ring were measured incorrectly, the contact pressure
would be aﬀected as well as the ﬁt pressure and the hoop stresses in the ring.
Knowing the exact parameter values is therefore important. This has not been
achieved for all the tests clearly demonstrated when using an incorrect width
when calculating the contact pressure in Test 8.
A critical stage of a test is to deﬁne when it fails. The failure criteria were in
this thesis deﬁned when concluding Test 2 as a sudden signiﬁcant increase in
running noise. This failure criteria proved to be suﬃcient for the tests which
suddenly developed one large pit. However, it proved diﬃcult to use when
many small pits were developed over many hours. Not having a common fai-
lure criteria for all the tests greatly inﬂuences the reliability of the individual
lifetimes.
The numerical analysis performed for the thesis is not satisfactory as all the
intended models are not ﬁnished and the models which are created does not
provide all the wanted solutions. The results from the example model give a
contact pressure which does match the analytical estimate. It also provides an
estimate of the von Mises stresses. However, the maximum contact pressure
was already known from the analytical calculations, and the contact pressure
==PAGE:117==
7.2. DISCUSSION
97
distribution could have easily been calculated as well. The stresses which were
originally sought was the shear stresses. The von Mises stress does show a
peak value below the surface as with the shear stress. However, the desired
stress distribution is not depicted by the von Mises stress. The reason behind
including the results was to show the models created in COMSOL which with
an appropriate time frame could have provided the desired results with regard
to the static case. However due to multiple diﬃculties with the program and
delays with the experimental part the models were never properly ﬁnished.
Creating Models 2 and 3 with their intended features was a far greater task
than anticipated and would require much attention to achieve.
==PAGE:118==
==PAGE:119==
8 Conclusion
RCF in a rolling element bearing can result in premature failure.
A wear
mechanism often observed is pitting. A hypothesis is that slip between rolling
element inﬂuences RCF and causes pitting to occur faster inducing premature
failure. This project aimed to investigate and collect data about the impact
of slip on RCF. To achieve the aim, it was desired to conduct RCF tests on a
test rig as well as create a numerical model.
The basic framework for conducting RCF tests with bearing rings have been
established including: instructions to start and stop the test rig; basis for cal-
culating the initial start parameters and data treatment scripts in Matlab®.
The modiﬁed rig can be eﬀectively used for studies of RCF testing of bearing
steels under well-controlled conditions. Eight initial tests have been performed
through which many of the possible pitfalls have been revealed:
• Misalignment in ground contact surface (conical shape).
• Misalignment when mounting test rings.
• Inaccurate measurements:
– Diameter measurement prior to mounting.
– Diametric expansion when mounting.
– Contact surface width.
• Insuﬃcient test failure criteria.
Though inconsistencies were present, high load RCF testing resulted in pit-
ting failure at the contacting surfaces. Two general pitting formations were
observed: a large localised main pit or evenly distributed uniform pits around
the entire circumference. The large pits were observed in tests with a contact
pressure assumably above 4 GPa (misaligned tests). All pits seem to originate
from the circumferential groove of the contact.
Although a limited number of tests were performed and slip was never intro-
duced a Weibull probability plot was created to interpret test lifetimes with
99
==PAGE:120==
100
CHAPTER 8. CONCLUSION
a contact pressure of 3.5 GPa. A narrow lifetime variability was found as the
slope of the ﬁtted line was 5.11. At 2.04 · 106 rev 63.2% of the tests would
have failed indicated by the Weibull scale parameter. Hypothetically introdu-
cing slip would have reduced the slope of the ﬁtted line and the Weibull scale
parameter thereby widening the lifetime variability and reducing the overall
lifetime.
The numerical modelling resulted in two models; an example model converted
to resemble Test 7 and a model to simulate the actual geometry of Test 7
more closely. The ﬁrst model resulted in a pressure distribution which ap-
proximately resembles the analytically calculated parameters of Test 7 as well
as a stress distribution. In the second model, symmetry conditions were not
utilised, facilitating rotation in a later model. Although the obtained results
did not match Test 7 as close as the example model, it was demonstrated that
the model was able to run without the symmetry conditions. Furthermore,
the initial steps of performing a mesh conversion study were initiated through
a parametric sweep. However, the model was never ﬁnished.
The overall aim of the project was not achieved as slip was never introduced in
the work. However when accounting for the experienced delays, the drafting
of the symposium paper and pitfalls the aim was never possible to achieve
within this time frame. That being said a solid baseline for future work has
been obtained.
8.1
Future Work
• Conduct further tests with a 3.5 GPa contact pressure based on this
thesis taking into account the discovered pitfalls. A 3.5 GPa contact
pressure produced a failure acceptably fast and produced an even da-
mage on the entire circumference facilitating the introduction of slip
fairly easy.
• Establishing a trustworthy Weibull probability plot by performing more
test with a 3.5 GPa contact pressure.
• Based on the deﬁnition of SRR calculate and implement slip into the
3.5 GPa tests.
• Create an additional Weibull probability plot for the slip tests and com-
pare them to the previous Weibull probability plot.
• Create a numerical model in COMSOL with all the desired aspects. A
standalone project is needed.
• To obtain identical start parameters a more consistent way of mounting
the rings is needed.
==PAGE:121==
8.1. FUTURE WORK
101
• A more measurable way of determining test ring failure than the noise
emitted from the test rig is needed. An example can be to measure the
level of vibrations.
• Rebuild the oil injection system on the test rig to facilitate adjustment
of the amount of oil introduced to the contact.
• As described in Janakiraman [1] investigating the eﬀect of varying hoop
stresses with the new test rings could be relevant.

The calculations are based on the heaviest loaded roller calculations. Here it was found that the bearing 
closely resemble a roller bearing, but the geometry is described as a spherical roller bearing.
Because the inner ring has proven to have the largest contact pressure the load on the heaviest loaded 
roller is calculated from this contact.
Both of the bearing rings and the rollers are made from the same steel:
The effective modulus of elasticity:
The total number of rollers in one row of the spherical roller bearing: 
The diameter of the roller is:
The effective length of the roller is approximated to:
The diametral clearance is found in the SKF rolling bearing catalogue in Table 4 on page 892. The 
radial internal clearance class C2 is chosen. It must be in the range between 20 and 40 micro meters, 20 
micro meters are used to have as small radial clearance as possible. 
According to Hamrock ch. 21, rollers in spherical roller bearings as seen in Fig. 21.7a has the following
To estimate the curvature radii of the inner and outer ring two different approaches are used. 
First the width and hight of the circle segment inside the outer ring is measured and from this the radius
are calculated:
Hight 1.6 mm
Width 26.2 mm
0.05442812500
On page 14 in Hamrock ch. 21 it is stated that the conformity ratio most commonly is 0.52, but must be 
between 0.51 and 0.54. For the outer ring it is:

beregninger er at eftervise hvad pasningstrykket bliver ved en diametral udvidelse på yderdiameteren 
på 0.12 mm.

Matlab scripts
Data treatment
The Matlab® scripts used for the data treatment is shown. The scripts are
shown in the order they are used. The scripts are descried in greater detail in
Section 5.3.
• Decimal_changer.m Changes decimal separator.
• FileImportToDbraw.m Imports raw data into databased ﬁle called dbraw.
• DataTreatmentRingPair8.m Uses raw data from dbraw8.m to create
presentable data.
• FinalDataTreatement.m Creates a scatter plot over the lifetime of each
test as well as creates a Weibull plot.

UDDEHOLM IMPAX SUPREME
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is a premium prehardened mould steel with
very good polishing and texturing properties.
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is available in a very wide dimensional range,
where even the largest dimensions show a very uniform hardness profile
all through the cross section.
The delivery hardness of ~310 HB, makes the steel suitable for a many
different applications like:
– Moulds for plastic injection moulding
– Moulds for blow moulding
– Dies for plastic extrusion
– General constructional parts, like machine components requiring
   improved fatigue strength and reliability
This information is based on our present state of knowledge and is intended to provide general
notes on our products and their uses. It should not therefore be construed as a warranty of
specific properties of the products described or a warranty for fitness for a particular purpose.

General
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is a premium-
quality vacuum-degassed Cr-Ni-Mo-alloyed
steel which is supplied in the hardened and
tempered condition, offering the following
benefits:
• No hardening risks
• No hardening costs
• Time saving, e.g. no waiting for heat
treatment
• Lower tool cost (e.g. no distortion to rectify)
• Modifications easily carried out
• Can be subsequently nitrided to increase
surface wear resistance or locally flame
hardened to reduce surface damage
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is manufactured to
consistently high quality standards with a very
low sulphur content, giving a steel with the
following characteristics:
• Good polishing and photo-etching properties
• Good machinability
• High purity and good homogeneity
• Uniform hardness
Note: Uddeholm Impax Supreme is 100%
ultrasonic tested.
Heavier sections are supplied premachined
which offers the following advantages com-
pared with un-machined material:
• Saving of weight
• Non-decarburized surface
• Exact nominal size (plus tolerance)
• Less machining
• Absence of scale minimizes machine and
tool wear
Properties
Physical data
Applications
• Injection moulds for thermoplastics
• Extrusion dies for thermoplastics
• Blow moulds
• Forming tools, press-brake dies (possibly
flame hardened or nitrided)
• Aluminium die casting prototype dies
• Structural components, shafts
Mechanical properties
Impact strength, tensile strength and the com-
pressive strength depends on the hardness in
the delivered condition.

Hardness: 325 HB.
Heat treatment
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is intended for use
in the hardened and tempered condition, i.e.
the delivery condition.
When, however, the steel is to be heat
treated to a higher hardness or case hardened,
the following instructions may be helpful.
Soft annealing
Protect the steel and heat through to 700°C
(1300°F). Then cool in the furnace at 10°C
(50°F)  per hour to 600°C (1110°F), then
freely in air.
Stress relieving
After rough machining the tool should be
heated through to 550°C (1020°F), holding
time 2 hours. Cool slowly to room tempera-
ture.
Hardening
Note: The steel should be fully soft annealed
before hardening.
Preheating temperature: 500–600°C (930–
1110°F).
Austenitizing temperature: 850°C (1560°F).
The steel should be heated through to the
austenitizing temperature and held at tempera-
ture for 30 minutes.
Protect the tool against decarburization and
oxidation during the hardening process.
Quenching media
• High speed gas/circulating atmosphere
(Only suitable for small dimensions)
• Oil (60–80°C/140–175°F)
• Martempering bath 300°C (570°F)
max. 4 minutes, then air
Note: Temper immediately tool reaches
50–70°C (120–160°F).
Tempering
Choose the tempering temperature according
to the hardness required by reference to the
tempering graph. Temper twice with inter-
mediate cooling to room temperature. Lowest
tempering temperature 180°C (360°F) for
small inserts, but preferred minimum is 250°C
(480°F). Holding time at temperature minimum
2 hours.

Nitriding and nitrocarburizing
Nitriding gives a hard surface which is very
resistant to wear and erosion. A nitrided
surface also increases the corrosion resistance.
For best result the following steps should be
followed:
1. Rough machining
2. Stress tempering at 550°C (1020°F)
3. Grinding
4. Nitriding
Following surface hardness and nitriding depths
will be achieved after nitriding:

Further information
Contact your local Uddeholm office for
further information on the selection, heat
treatment, application and availability of Udde-
holm tool steels, including the publication
“Steels for moulds”.
Welding
Good results when welding tool steel can be
achieved if proper precautions are taken
during welding (elevated working temperature,
joint prep-aration, choice of consumables and
welding procedure). If the tool is to be pol-
ished or photo-etched, it is necessary to work
with an electrode type of matching composi-
tion.

Electrical-discharge
machining
If spark-erosion, EDM, is performed in the as
delivered condition, the tool should then be
given an additional temper at approx. 550°C
(1020°F). If the steel has been rehardened, the
additional tempering temperature should be
25°C (50°F) lower than the last tempering
temperature used.
Further information can be obtained from
the Uddeholm brochure “EDM of tool steel”.
Hard-chromium-plating
After hard-chromium-plating, the tool should
be tempered for approx. 4 hours at 180°C
(350°F) within 4 hours of plating in order to
avoid hydrogen embrittlement.
Photo-etching
Uddeholm Impax Supreme is particularly
suitable for texturing by the photo-etching
process. Its very low sulphur content ensures
accurate and consistent pattern reproduction.
For heavy sections an extra tempering at
550°C (1020°F) before photo-etching is
recommended.
Polishing
Uddeholm Impax Supreme has good polish-
ability in the hardened and tempered condi-
tion. After grinding, polishing is undertaken
with aluminium oxide or diamond paste.
Note: Each steel grade has an optimum polish-
ing time which largely depends on hardness
and polishing technique. Overpolishing can
lead to a poor surface finish (e.g. an “orange
peel” effect).
Further information is given in the Udde-
holm publication “Polishing of mould steel”.

English
Mobil Delvac Synthetic Gear Oil 75W-140  
Commercial Vehicle Lube Mobil, United Kingdom
Fully Synthetic Heavy Duty Gear Lubricant
Product Description
Mobil Delvac Synthetic Gear Oil 75W-140 is a fully synthetic drivetrain lubricant engineered to meet demanding performance requirements including Scania
STO 1:0, Scania STO 2:0 A and API GL-5. This product is designed for use in heavy-duty drivetrains that require gear lubricants with relatively high viscosity
and excellent load-carrying capability and where extreme pressures and shock loading are expected. Mobil Delvac Synthetic Gear Oil incorporates synthetic
basestock and advanced additives providing significant advantages over mineral gear oils.
The state-of-the-art technology in Mobil Delvac Synthetic Gear Oil 75W-140 excellent performance for wide temperature range application, helps to
protect against thermal degradation and oxidation and contributes to reduced wear and corrosion, improved shear stability and fuel economy.
It is formulated to provide an inherently higher viscosity index using synthetic base oils providing stronger film strength at higher temperatures than mineral
oils. This technology also ensures effective lubrication at below freezing temperatures without channelling.
 
Features and Benefits
Today’s technology has vastly improved the performance capabilities of heavy-duty on and off-highway equipment in terms of load, torque, speed, control,
and reliability through innovative drivetrain designs. These designs have markedly changed and increased the requirements of lubricants to deliver this higher
level of performance, increase productivity, and reduce operating costs. For heavy duty drivetrains, friction control, wear protection, thermal stability, shear
stability, rust and corrosion prevention, and seal protection are features that must be optimally balanced to contribute to extended gear and seal life, smooth
operation and high load high torque capability over a wide range of applications and operating environments. The key benefits include:
Features
Potential Benefits
Exceptional thermal stability and resistance to high temperature oxidation
Long gear and bearing life due to minimal deposits 
Long seal life
Outstanding protection against low speed/high torque wear and high
speed scoring
Increased load-carrying capability. 
Reduced maintenance costs and long equipment life
Exceptional shear stability
Retains viscosity and film strength under severe operating conditions to
prevent wear
Outstanding low temperature fluidity versus mineral oils
Reduced wear and ease of start-up
Good resistance to foaming
Maintains film strength for reliable lubrication
Compatible with typical automotive seals and gaskets
Minimum leakage and reduced contamination
 
Applications
       Recommended by ExxonMobil for use in:
Heavy duty manual transmissions, axles and final drives requiring API GL-5 and Scania STO 1:0 & 2:0 A performance
On highway light and heavy duty trucks, busses and vans
Off highway industries including: construction, mining, quarrying, and agriculture
Other heavy duty industrial gear drives including hypoid and worm gears operating under conditions where high speed/shock load, high speed/low
torque, and/or low speed/high torque conditions prevail

Cylinder Roller Contact
CYLINDER ROLLER COONTACT
161
Cylinder Roller Coontact
==PAGE:182==
2 |  C Y L I N D E R  R O L L E R  C O N T A C T
Introduction
Consider an infinitely long steel cylinder resting on a flat aluminum foundation, where 
both structures are elastic. The cylinder is subjected to a point load along its top. The 
objective of this study is to find the contact pressure distribution and the length of contact 
between the foundation and the cylinder. An analytical solution exists, and this tutorial 
includes a comparison with the COMSOL Multiphysics solution. The application is based 
on a NAFEMS benchmark (see Ref. 1).
Model Definition
This is a plane strain problem and the 2D Solid Mechanics interface from the Structural 
Mechanics Module is thus suitable. The 2D geometry is further cut in half at the vertical 
symmetry axis.
Figure 1: Model geometry.
In 2D, the cylinder is subjected to a point load along its top with an intensity of 35 kN/
mm. Both the cylinder and block material are elastic, homogeneous, and isotropic.
The contact modeling method in this example only includes the frictionless part of the 
example described in Ref. 1. This model uses a contact pair, which is a straightforward way 
to implement a contact problem using the Solid Mechanics interface.
162
RELEVANT TEXTS
==PAGE:183==

In these equations, E1 and E2 are Young’s modulus of the roller and the block, 
respectively, and R1 is the radius of the roller. Combining these equations results in a 
contact length of 6.21 mm and a maximum contact pressure of 3585 MPa.
Figure 3 depicts the contact pressure along the contact area for both the analytical and the 
COMSOL Multiphysics solution.
Figure 3: Analytical pressure distribution (solid line) and COMSOL Multiphysics solution 
(dashed line).
Notes About the COMSOL Implementation
The Structural Mechanics Module supports contact boundary conditions using contact 
pairs. The contact pair is defined by a source boundary and a destination boundary. The 
destination boundary is the one which is coupled to the source boundary if contact is 
established. The terms source and destination should be interpreted as in “the destination 
receives its displacements from the source.” As a result, the contact pressure variable is 
available on the destination boundary. The mesh on the destination side should always be 
finer than on the source side.
In this example, the contact boundary pair consists of a flat source boundary and a curved 
destination boundary.
164
RELEVANT TEXTS
==PAGE:185==
5 |  C Y L I N D E R  R O L L E R  C O N T A C T
To reduce the number of iteration steps and improve convergence, it is good practice to 
set an initial contact pressure as close to the anticipated solution as possible. A good 
approximation is to use the value of the external pressure—in this case the external point 
load divided by an estimated contact length and the thickness. In this example, it is 
necessary to specify an initial contact pressure to make the model stable with respect to the 
initial conditions, because the initial configuration—where the cylinder is free to move in 
the vertical direction—is singular. An alternative could be to define the geometries with a 
small overlap or supporting the roller with weak springs.
The small size of the contact region necessitates a local mesh refinement. Use a free mesh 
for the cylindrical domain and a mapped mesh for the aluminum block. The block 
geometry requires some modification to set up a refined mesh area.
The solver sequence set up as default by the program for a contact problem is a segregated 
solution, with displacements and contact pressures solved separately. The solver settings 
for the contact pressure step give optimal quality and should usually not be modified.
References
1. A.W.A. Konter, Advanced Finite Element Contact Benchmarks, NAFEMS, 2006.
2. M.A. Crisfield, Non-linear Finite Element Analysis of Solids and Structures, volume 
2: Advanced Topics, John Wiley & Sons, London, 1997.

Status Report 1
Page 1
1
Intro
The purpose of this status report is to outline how tests are set up and carried out on the test rig
shown in Figure 1.1. Additionally status report 1 strives to brieﬂy explain the choices made for
the tests related to the ﬁrst ring pair tested, discuss the ﬁndings with regard to the durability of
the rings and state the changes and improvements to be made for coming tests.
The status report is drafted as the ﬁrst test series has been completed. The main focus for
these tests has been to establish the running parameters for the test rig and learn how to best
operate the rig for future tests. It was also desired to investigate how the bearing rings performed
when subjected to a given load level. The following section will outline the preliminary steps
performed in order to set up the ﬁrst test series.
(a) Overview of the test rig showing the diﬀer-
ent components in relation to each other.
(b) Closeup of the test space in the rig, show-
ing how the rings are mounted in relation to
each other.
Figure 1.1: Test rig used in the project.
2
Preliminary steps - 1st test series
Below are shown the initial steps.
• Inner rings were removed from two SKF Explorer 21311 EK + H 311 spherical roller
bearings. The bearing cross section can be seen in Figure 2.1a.
1. The ﬁrst inner ring was removed by cutting the outer ring in half.
2. The second inner ring was removed by breaking the roller cage and removing enough
rollers to get the inner ring loose.
This method will be used for the remaining
bearings.
• The contact area on one ring was ground to a width of 4 mm to avoid two opposing edges
rolling against each other, see Figure 2.1b. The second ring was left untouched with a
contact area width of 2 mm. A cross section of the intended contact can be seen in Figure
2.1b.
182
RELEVANT TEXTS
==PAGE:203==
Status Report 1
Page 2
• Both rings were pressed onto their respective shafts till a diametrically expansion of 0.12
mm was achieved on the outer diameter of the rings.
This expansion approximately
corresponds to 136 MPa when rotating 2000 rpm at 20°C. The calculation performed is
shown in Appendix A, these calculations show the contact pressure if the expansion had
been measured at the ﬁt radius. This is discussed further below Table 2.1.
• Both shafts were installed in the test rig and their positions were adjusted using shim
washers and wave springs. This was done to get about 1 mm of clearance one each side
of the untouched ring to avoid the edges of the rings touching.
• The desired contact pressure for the ﬁrst test series was 1 GPa, which corresponds to
1034.56 N of force on the rings, calculated using a simpliﬁed analytical solution by
Hamrock. However due to the error in the calculations the actual values are 1.42 GPa and
2079 N based on the 2 mm contact width. The calculation relating to the 1 GPa case is
shown in Appendix B.
• The rotational speed of the shaft was set to approximately 2000 rpm.
• To investigate the eﬀect of torque on the rings, the ﬁrst test series has been preformed with
emphasis’s on minimizing the torque transferred.
• The sensors measuring the rotational speed of the two shafts were checked with a hand-
held tachometer prior to the tests. The tachometer used had the serial number 150121649
and can be seen in Figure 2.2a.
• The torque sensor was tested using a wrench and a 2 kg mass to apply a known static
torque on the shaft, which could be compared to the readout in LabVIEW. The setup is
shown in Figure 2.2b.
While writing this text some typing errors were found in the calculations. This changed the
intended contact pressure and the ﬁt pressure. The actual values are shown in the list above
and the corresponding calculations in the appendix have been corrected. Table 2.1 show a
comparison between the values based on the incorrect calculations, the values that was actually
applied in the test and the values that were originally intended.
(a) Cross section of SKF Explorer 21311 EK + H
311.
(b) Cross section of the inner rings in the
intended contact.
Figure 2.1: Sketches of bearings being investigated in the project.1

The actual ﬁt pressure shown in Table 2.1 has proven to be very strong. To remove the rings
from the shafts after the test series, approximately 120 kN force were applied in the hydraulic
press onto the side of the test rings. As shown in Appendix A a diametric expansion of 0.12
mm at the ﬁt radius was originally intended. Thus the outside diameter should not have been
expanded more than 0.089 mm. Because the expansion of 0.12 mm was measured on the outside
diameter the ﬁt pressure increased from 100.28 MPa to 136 MPa. The strength of a ﬁt pressure
of approximately 100 MPa would have been strong enough, however due to the ﬁrst test series
having been carried out using 136 MPa ﬁt pressure it has been decided not to change this for the
coming test series. The reason for this is a desire to only change one parameter at a time. The
outer ring diameter will thus still be expanded 0.12 mm when mounting future test rings.

Operating the test rig - 1st test series
To start the test rig the following sequence was used.
• The rings are brought together while turning one shaft manually to be able to see when the
other shaft begin to rotate slightly. When the shafts touch (which means that the contact
pressure is not great enough to rotate the not-spinning shaft) the force zero point is set.
• The zero point for the force is set by adjusting "Intersect Y pressure scale" till "Force
between testdiscs [N]" is approximately 0.
• While both shafts are at a standstill "Intersect Y Torque scale" is adjusted till "Torque on
shaft (Drive 2)" is approximately 0.
• The values of all parameters are noted in the manual logbook.
• "Pumpe olieﬁlter" is started.
• "Pumpe smøreolie" is started.
• "Pumpe oliekøler" is started.
• "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" are started simultaneously at 10% and 10.5%
respectively.
• The automatic log is started. This is set to record all values once every second.
• The pressure in the hydraulic loading arrangement is now brought up to approximately
2.6 bar, bringing "Force between testdiscs [N]" up to approximately 2079 N.
• The rotational speed of "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" are increased to 70 % and 71
% respectively which correspond to about 2000 rpm. The 1% oﬀset is due to the diﬀerent
diameters of the rings.
The manual logbook mentioned above has been introduced to ensure that the test rig is operated
consistently. The logbook states the initial conditions from the LabVIEW program monitoring
the rig, the order at which diﬀerent parts of the rig are started and stopped and manual recorded
readings from LabVIRW at a half-hourly interval.
An example of a logbook is shown in
Appendix C.
To stop the test rig the following order has been used.
• The pressure in the hydraulic loading arrangement is reduced thus bringing "Force between
testdiscs [N]" below 0 and the two rings out of contact.
• "Motor drive 1" and "Motor drive 2" are stopped simultaneously.
• The automatic log is stopped.
• "Pumpe smøreolie" is stopped.
• "Pumpe oliekøler" is stopped.
• "Pumpe olieﬁlter" is stopped.
STATUS REPORT 1
185
==PAGE:206==
Status Report 1
Page 5
As seen in the example logbook the order at which the rig is stopped has changed. The reason
being that reducing the rotational speed of each drive before taking the rings out of contact
introduced unnecessary and unwanted ﬂuctuations in the torque.
The manual logbook will mainly be used to keep the consistency in the test series, it can however
also be used to quickly compare two diﬀerent data sets.
The data obtained from automatic logging the test rig is on the following form:
Time;Hydraulic pressure [Bar];Torque [Nm];Torque Mean [Nm];RPM Drive 1;...
1,000000;100,000000;0,100000;0,100000;2000,000000;...
2,000000;100,000000;0,100000;0,100000;2000,000000;...
3,000000;100,000000;0,100000;0,100000;2000,000000;...
4,000000;100,000000;0,100000;0,100000;2000,000000;...
.
.
.
Due to the width of the page not all parameters could be shown above. The parameters being
recorded in the log are as follows:
1. Time
2. Hydraulic pressure [Bar]
3. Torque [Nm]
4. Torque Mean [Nm]
5. RPM Drive 1
6. RPM mean Drive 1
7. RPM Drive 2
8. RPM mean Drive 2
9. Counter drive 1
10. Counter drive 2
Originally "Oil temperature (C°)" was also recorded, but at the time of writing this, it was
not possible to include since the module connecting the sensor to LabVIEW is not available.
However while performing the tests it has been noted that the steel pipes between the oil drip
tray and the oil tank only feel a little warm, even after seven hours of running the test rig.
In the next section the data obtained during the ﬁrst test series will be presented and discussed
and changes for the next tests will be put forward.

4
Findings and changes - 1st test series
After completing all the tests with ring pair one, minor changes had to be performed to each of
the data ﬁles before continuing with the data treatment. These changes included:
1. Removing data points at the end, after the parameter "Hydraulic pressure [Bar]" dropped
below 0.
2. Removing "Oil temperature (C°)" from the ﬁrst row.
3. Removing ";;;" at the end of the ﬁrst row.
4. Changing the decimal separator from "," to ".".
Some of the above was performed manually but changing the decimal separator was done with
a MATLAB script in MATLAB R2016a. The script is shown in Appendix D.
The data ﬁles was then imported into a database ﬁle called "dbraw.mat" using another MATLAB
script. This script is shown in Appendix E.
Finally the database ﬁle was loaded into a data treatment script which has been used to calculate
the values shown in Table 4.1 as well as produced the plots shown in Figures 4.3, 4.4 and 4.5.
The script is shown in Appendix F.
Because of an error while setting up LabVIEW for test run 8, automatic logging did not
occur. According to the manual logbook the missing data corresponds to 3 hours and 7 minutes.
When comparing the manual logbook to the one from test run 7 it is seen that only 2 minutes
separate the two, which considering the overall test series length is next to nothing. One can
also observe that the manual recorded readouts match each other closely. Therefore the data
from test run 7 is included twice in the data treatment, to avoid loosing the loading history from
test run 8. A comparison of the two is included in Appendix G.

STATUS REPORT 1
187
==PAGE:208==
Status Report 1
Page 7
is the reason for stopping the ﬁrst test series at this point. Another reason for stopping at this
point is that the two contacting surfaces appear not to have been perfectly aligned. This can
clearly be seen on Figure 4.1, here the edge labelled "Rolling contact" is clearly more worn
than "2nd rolling contact". It is possible that this misalignment has occurred since the middle
section in the bearing is not a working surface and thus is not required to be parallel to the
bearing axis i.e. not as tight tolerances as the working surfaces on either side. At the total
number of revolutions seen in Table 4.1 the ring has been worn suﬃciently to reach contact
with the other edge. Because of this misalignment the pressure in the contact has been greater
than anticipated in the calculations. From Figure 4.1 an approximate contact width of 0.4 mm
is assumed, combining this with the mean force shown in Table 4.1, it is found that the mean
contact pressure has been 3.19 GPa with peak values as high as 3.73 GPa. The calculations can
be seen Appendix H. Because of this unintended change in contact pressure the usefulness of
this speciﬁc test series is reduced. However because of the increased contact pressure it was
found that the originally intended contact pressure would not have been able to damage the rings
as fast. Therefore the contact pressure will be increased to 3.5 GPa for the coming tests to lower
the duration of the test series. Corresponding calculations is shown in Appendix I. In an eﬀort
to avoid the misalignment for future tests both rings will be ground. Despite the errors of this
ﬁrst test series, it still fulﬁlled its main purpose; to establish the running parameters for the test
rig and learn how to best operate the rig during future tests.

In Figure 4.3 one can see relatively large ﬂuctuations in the applied force. The steep increases
seen periodically is due to start-up and stopping of the rig. However despite an attempt to set
the force at the target line at the beginning of each test, the force increased rapidly past the target
line within the ﬁrst 5 minutes of running. This increase comes from an increase in temperature,
and thus is seen to even out when the stable operating temperature is reached. This eﬀect was
hard to anticipate in the ﬁrst tests however became easier to control as experience with the test
rig was gained. This is evident from the decreasing size of ﬂuctuations as time increase. The
target line shown is the target based on the incorrect calculations, it is however the target used
during the test series and is thus displayed here.
Figure 4.4: The torque transmitted plottet against time.
Figure 4.4 show rather large ﬂuctuations in the transmitted torque. Because of this it has been
hard to control the torque satisfactory. It can be seen that the torque has been lowered over
time and during the last tests has been as low as deemed possible. The reason for bringing the
torque down this slowly is that Drive 1 is not allowed to transmit torque to Drive 2. If this was
to happen there is a risk of ruining Drive 2 because it is not set up to receive power via the
shaft. If a negative torque value is seen in Figure 4.4 then Drive 1 is transmitting torque to Drive
2. The large ﬂuctuations is thought to occur because the rings are not perfectly round. Again
this can be because the original ring does not posses perfect roundness in the middle section
that is utilized here. Another reason for the ﬂuctuations could be the grinding machine used.
According to the workshop it has not been used for some time, and thus cannot grind the rings
to the roundness needed. The workshop is at the time of writing in the process of renovating the
grinding machine to ensure better grinding for future test rings.

(a) Rotational speed Drive 1 against time.
(b) Rotational speed Drive 2 against time.
Figure 4.5: Rotational speed plottet against time.
Figures 4.5a and 4.5b show the rotational speed of each drive respectively. As can be seen the
rotational speeds have been roughly identical which is desired in order to have rolling without
sliding.
The ﬂuctuation seen will hopefully decrees as better roundness is achieved.
The
rotational speed has been higher than originally planed, this will be adjusted for the coming
tests.
4.1
Changes to be made
These are the changes intended for the coming test series.
• The grinding machine will be renovated.
• The accumulator on the test rig will be inspected and possibly adjusted to a more ﬁtting
pressure.
• Both rings will be ground.
1. The ﬁrst ring will have a contact area width of 3 mm.
2. The second ring will have a contact area width of 5 mm.
• Both rings will be mounted on their respective shafts using a cone interference ﬁt with
approximately 136 MPa of ﬁt pressure.
• Before and after a test series each ring will be checked for the following:
1. roundness.
2. surface roughness.
3. exact diameter.
• During tests:
1. The torque will be kept more constant around zero as the safe range of operation has
been established.
2. The force controlling the contact pressure will ideally be kept within a range of ±50
N.
3. The contact pressure will be increased to 3.5 GPa (19,010 N applied).
4. The rolling speed of Drive 2 will be lowered closer to 2000 rpm.

Status Report 1

1
Intro
This report will document the procedure during the second test series. The object of the second
test series, which was the ﬁrst real test, has been to perform pure rolling contact fatigue on the
ring pair using a contact pressure of 3.5 GPa.
2
Preliminary steps - 2nd test series
The steps taken to set up the second test series are shown below.
• Both inner rings were removed from the original bearings as stated in Status Report 1.
• Both rings were ground.
1. The ﬁrst was ground to a 3 mm wide contact face, resulting in a diameter of 75.84
mm.
2. The second was ground to a 5 mm wide contact face, resulting in a diameter of 75.33
mm.
• The rings were labelled as "2-1" and "2-2" using an electric-spark engraving pen. See
Figure 2.1
1. The ﬁrst number represent the test series.
2. The second number represent the drive on which each ring was to be mounted.
• The surface roughness was tested across the width of ring 2-1, the ring with a 5 mm
wide contact face. See the measured value in Table 2.1. The surface roughness was only
measured on ring 2-1 because ring 2-2 was to narrow.
• The rings were mounted on their receptive shafts. Both rings expanded 0.12 mm as in the
ﬁrst test series giving the following ﬁt pressures,
1. Ring 2-1: 131.83 MPa. See Appendix A.
2. Ring 2-2: 134.98 MPa. See Appendix B.
• The shafts were mounted in the test rig and aligned using the same shim washers and
wave springs as in the ﬁrst test series. The center of each ring was not perfectly aligned
however the 3 mm contact face was within the 5 mm contact face and thus the alignment
was accepted.
• Before positioning the shafts at their running positions in the test rig, the roundness was
tested with a
1
100 mm dial indicator. See Table 2.1
• The shafts were positioned and the drives connected.
As can be seen in the list above there are some divination in the ﬁt pressure due to the diﬀerent
diameter of the two rings. The reason for these values not being more equal is that it is not
possible to make the small alterations to the expansion that would be needed to ﬁne tune the ﬁt
pressure further.

195
==PAGE:216==
Status Report 1
Page 2
Figure 2.1: The engraving on the test rings.
Note that ring 2-2 appear to be worn on the surface on which the engraving has been performed.
This is due to the ring being pressed against a spacing pipe, which is placed with a loose ﬁt.

3
Operating the test rig - 2nd test series
The procedure to start the test rig described in Status Report 1 section 3 has been used throughout
the second test series with minor adjustments which are the following:
• After starting drives 1 and 2 the speed is increased to approximately 2000 rpm.
• Logging is started.
• An initial line contact is created of approximately 1.5 GPa in the contact area. This is the
level at which the fast increase in pressure evens out, because of the pre set pressure in the
accumulator.
• The torque is adjusted as close to zero as possible by adjusting the frequency of the
individual drives.
196
RELEVANT TEXTS
==PAGE:217==
Status Report 1
Page 3
• The contact pressure is increased to 3.5 GPa corresponding to 18.937 N provided by the
hydraulic pressure arrangement. See Appendix C.
To stop the test rig, the exact same procedure as described in Status Report 1 section 3 has been
used.
The LabVIEW program used in the ﬁrst test series has been used again. The log from the
program is thus the same and has the same structure as shown in Status Report 1 section 3 and
will therefore not be presented.
During the ﬁrst start-up of the test rig with the second ring pair, the hydraulic loading arrange-
ment was found to be less sensitive then previously experienced. The pressure appeared to
return to approximately 3 bar after each pump stroke. Because of this the test was stopped. The
reason for the slow increase in pressure is probably the accumulator. More speciﬁcally the pre
set pressure in the accumulator which is not known, but must be in the region of 3 bar. When a
higher pressure then 3 bar is needed in the hydraulic loading arrangement both the piston and
the accumulator must be ﬁlled with oil, this is not the case at pressures lower then 3 bar. The
increased volume needed to be ﬁlled with oil causes a reduced pressure built up speed thus
increasing the time to reach the operating contact pressure between the test rings. The overall
stability of the hydraulic loading arrangement seem to be more stable at higher pressures. This is
likely because of the increased oil volume in the hydraulic loading arrangement. The increased
oil volume makes the system more resistant to bleeding in the valves, which is the source of
pressure loss in the hydraulic loading arrangement. Because of the increased stability in the
hydraulic loading arrangement no changes will be made to it.
During the second attempt to start up the rig, a continues graph in the LabVIEW program was
not updating. The rig was stopped because it was unclear if data was being collected. After
restarting LabVIEW the test rig was started without problems. Data from both the ﬁrst attempt
and the second attempt are included in the data treatment.
The status of the ring pair was checked after 5 hours of running. The only noteworthy change
at this point was a line which looked like a small continues cut along the contact path of one
edge. After 20 hours and 47 minutes of testing, a loud banging noise was observed and the test
rig was shut down as fast as possible. The source of the noise was a large pit in surface of ring
2-1. See Figure 3.1. The pit seem to have started from the edge contact close to the 2 mm mark
on the ruler and then moved towards the middle of the contact. As with the ﬁrst test series the
contact seem to have been mainly along the line by the 2 mm mark seen in Figure 3.1. A more
faint line can be seen to develop in the opposite side, this however does not seem to have been
in contact for as long as the line to the right. The area between the lines does not seem to have
been worn, this could mean that the area did not contact the other ring. This will however need
to be investigated closer using microscopes.

197

Status Report 1

Figure 3.1: The pit developed in ring 2-1.

To perform ﬁnal data treatment the MATLAB script shown in Appendix D has been used. This
is roughly the same script as used during the ﬁrst test series, but with minor alterations to the
plots created. The data shown in Table 4.1 was calculated in the script as well.

Table 4.1 show the time the ring pair has been in contact under testing conditions, the mean of
each adjustable parameter as well as the surface roughness of each ring after the test series. As
198
RELEVANT TEXTS
==PAGE:219==
Status Report 1
Page 5
was seen with the ﬁrst ring pair, drive 1 has been rotating slightly faster then drive 2 due to a
smaller diameter of the ring. The total number of revolutions has exceeded 106 which, when
considering a SN curve for 100Cr6 bearing steel, indicate that the test rings are in the high cycle
fatigue range. Also considering the SKF rolling bearing catalogue which state that a load of 4
GPa was required to produce a permanent indentation of
1
10,000 of the rolling element diameter
into the raceway. The contact pressure of 3.5 GPa is thus not large enough to produce plastic
yielding in the test rings however it is high enough to produce failure within an acceptable time
frame, while still being in the high cycle fatigue range. The results obtained during the tests are
thus representative for high cycle fatigue in roller bearings.
Figure 4.1: The applied contact force plottet against time.
In Figure 4.1 one can see periodical steep increases in the moving average of the force, these
occur when starting up and stopping the test rig. The average value is further below the target
then originally planed. This is believed to be due to the slow increase in force at the beginning
of each test, this can be seen when comparing the average to the moving average which is within
an acceptable range of the target force. The cumulative moving average tends to even out around
the average value also due to the start up and shut down of each test. The three peaks values
of the moving average deviate respectively 227.7 N, 177.7 N and 170.7 N above the target line,
which considering the magnitude of the applied force is an acceptable low deviation. The goal
of controlling the force within a 100 N range set in Status Report 1, is largely met as a large
portion of the moving average is within the range shown in Figure 4.1, however there is room
for improvement.

little to no eﬀect from the grinding of the rings with regards to the stability of the torque. The
applied torque seen in Figure 4.2 can however be considered low and when the applied torque is
raised in the coming tests it should be possible to clearly distinguish between the tests.

been decided when to implement this.

Status Report 1
